The Ruling on Salb: General Booty or Killer's Prize?
Defines salb and rules it is general spoils unless imam assigns it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on salb, distinguish it from the Shafi'i position, and cite the respective proofs.

Core Idea
Salb is part of general booty unless the imam grants it; the killer has no automatic right.

When a fighter kills an enemy in battle, does he automatically own the enemy's horse, weapons, and armor? The schools of law differ. In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi ruling based on a hadith from the Prophet ﷺ, the definition of what constitutes salb, and the response to the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
The passage addresses the legal status of salb (spoils taken from a slain enemy). The core question: does the salb automatically belong to the killer, or is it part of the general booty (ghanimah)?
2
State the Hanafi Position
The Hanafi school holds that if the imam has not specifically awarded the salb to the killer as a nafal (incentive), it is pooled with the rest of the ghanimah and divided among all entitled fighters.
The passage begins: 'And the salb is for all of it if he did not give extra incentive.'
3
Define the Components of Salb
The text defines what is included in salb: the slain's mount (with its saddle), clothes, weapons, and anything carried on the mount. Items like a slave on another mount or property at home are excluded.
4
Examine the Hanafi Proof
The proof is the hadith of Habib ibn Abi Salama: 'You have nothing from the salb of your killed except what your imam grants you.' This shows the salb is at the imam's discretion.
The hadith is quoted: 'ليس لك من سلب قتيلك إلا ما طابت به نفس إمامك'
5
Understand the Opposing View and Hanafi Response
Shafi'i and Ahmad argue that the killer deserves the salb automatically, citing the hadith 'Whoever kills an enemy, he is entitled to his salb.' The Hanafi response reconciles this by interpreting that hadith as referring to tanfil (imam granting the salb as an incentive), not automatic entitlement.
The thamarah al-khilaf: in the Shafi'i school, the killer takes the salb without permission; in the Hanafi school, it is divided unless the imam declares otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسلب للكل منها إن لم ينفل، وهو مركبه وثيابه وسلاحه وما معه.
السرية
الإمام به للقاتل أي السلب
= إلى الغزاة باعتبار أنهم من الفقراء؛ لأن المستحق فقير غير معين، فإذا جاز صرفه لفقير غير مقاتل فصرفه للفقير المقاتل أولى، وعند الشافعي ومالك: لا ينفل من الخمس أيضاً. رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٨/١] (فتح) فقط: أي لا من أربعة الأخماس. (ط، عيني) والسلب للكل منها أي إن لم يجعل الإمام سلب المقتول للقاتل، فهو من جملة الغنيمة، القاتل وغيره سواء فيه، فيقسم قسمة الغنائم عندنا؛ لقوله عليها الحبيب بن أبي سلمة: ليس" لك من سلب قتيلك إلا ما طابت به نفس إمامك"، وقال الشافعي: السلب للقاتل إذا كان له، وقد قتله مقبلاً؛ لقوله عليه: "من قتل قتيلاً أهل أن من فله سلبه"، ولأنه أكثر قتالا، فيختص به إظهاراً للتفاوت بينه وبين غيره وبه قال أحمد، ولنا: ما روينا، ولأنه مأخوذ بقوة جيش الإسلام، فكان غنيمة ،الإسلام، فوجب أن يقسم قسمة الغنائم وما رواه يحتمل التنفيل، فيحمل عليه توفيقاً بين الحديثين، وليس فيما رواه اشتراط القتل مقبلاً. (عيني، فتح)
يسهم
وهو مركبه [ أي مركب المقتول وما عليه من السرج والآلة. (ط، عيني)] أي يدخل في السلب مركبه مع السرج وثيابه وسلاحه وما معه على الدابة وما عدا ذلك فليس بسلب، وكذلك ما كان مع غلامه على دابة أخرى، وكذلك عبده وما في بيته رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٨/١]
And the salb is for all of it if he did not give extra incentive. And it is his mount, clothes, weapons, and what is with him...
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the status of salb (spoils taken from a slain enemy). In the Hanafi school, if the imam has not specifically assigned the salb to the killer (via tanfil), the salb is part of the general booty and is divided among all those entitled to spoils. The proof is the hadith of Habib ibn Abi Salama: 'You have nothing from the salb of your killed except what your imam grants you.' The Shafi'i school (and Ahmad) hold that the salb automatically belongs to the killer if he killed the enemy face-to-face, citing the hadith 'Whoever kills an enemy, he is entitled to his salb.' The Hanafi response is that this hadith refers to tanfil (imam's incentive), not automatic entitlement. The passage also details the contents of salb: mount, saddle, weapons, clothes, and what the slain has with him on his mount; but not his slave or items on another mount or at home.

Key Terms
سلب (salb): The spoils taken from a slain enemy: his mount, saddle, weapons, clothes, and what he carries on his mount.
غنيمة (ghanimah): Spoils of war taken from the enemy, subject to division per Islamic law.
نفل (nafal): An extra incentive or grant given by the imam to fighters, such as awarding a portion of the spoils beyond the normal share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT included in the definition of salb (spoils of a slain enemy)?
A) The slain's mount with saddle
B) The slain's clothes
C) A slave belonging to the slain who was on another mount
D) The slain's weapons

Key Takeaway
The salb of a slain enemy is part of the general booty (ghanimah) and is divided among all entitled fighters, unless the imam specifically awards it to the killer as an incentive (nafal).

Ruling on Slaba: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Dispute
Explanation of slaba and the dispute with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on slaba and explain the evidence and reconciliation with the Shafi'i proof.

Core Idea
The slaba belongs to the entire army unless the imam awards it to the killer.

This lesson explores a key dispute in the fiqh of jihad: does the personal effects (slaba) of a slain enemy belong automatically to the killer, or is it part of the general booty? The Hanafi school argues the latter, based on a hadith that gives discretion to the imam. We will examine the proofs and the method of reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the default ruling: slaba is part of booty unless the imam awards it as nafal.
The burden is on the imam's explicit award.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Shafi'i and Ahmad hold that the killer automatically gets the slaba if killed face-to-face.
Their view is based on a different hadith.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the hadith 'Whoever kills a slain person, he has a right to his slaba.' and the reasoning that the killer fights more.
This seems to directly grant the right.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school interprets the opponent's hadith as referring to nafal (discretionary award), thus reconciling both hadiths. Also note the absence of the condition 'facing' in the Hanafi proof.
The reconciliation method is common in Hanafi usul.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Hanafi law, the killer cannot claim the slaba automatically; under Shafi'i law, he can without imam's permission.
This affects how booty is collected and distributed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسلب للكل منها إن لم ينفل، وهو مركبه وثيابه وسلاحه وما معه. السرية الإمام به للقاتل أي السلب. والسلب للكل منها أي إن لم يجعل الإمام سلب المقتول للقاتل، فهو من جملة الغنيمة، القاتل وغيره سواء فيه، فيقسم قسمة الغنائم عندنا؛ لقوله عليه الحبيب بن أبي سلمة: ليس لك من سلب قتيلك إلا ما طابت به نفس إمامك، وقال الشافعي: السلب للقاتل إذا كان له، وقد قتله مقبلاً؛ لقوله عليه: من قتل قتيلاً له أن من فله سلبه، ولأنه أكثر قتالا، فيختص به إظهاراً للتفاوت بينه وبين غيره وبه قال أحمد، ولنا: ما روينا، ولأنه مأخوذ بقوة جيش الإسلام، فكان غنيمة الإسلام، فوجب أن يقسم قسمة الغنائم وما رواه يحتمل التنفيل، فيحمل عليه توفيقاً بين الحديثين، وليس فيما رواه اشتراط القتل مقبلاً. وهو مركبه أي مركب المقتول وما عليه من السرج والآلة. أي يدخل في السلب مركبه مع السرج وثيابه وسلاحه وما معه على الدابة وما عدا ذلك فليس بسلب، وكذلك ما كان مع غلامه على دابة أخرى، وكذلك عبده وما في بيته.
And the slaba is for all of them if he does not award it as nafal. It is his mount, his clothes, his weapon, and what is with him. The imam awards it to the killer, i.e., the slaba. And the slaba is for all of them, i.e., if the imam does not make the slaba of the slain person for the killer, then it is part of the booty, the killer and others are equal in it, so it is divided as booty according to us; because of his saying, peace be upon him, to Habib ibn Abi Salama: 'You have nothing from the slaba of your kill except that which your imam is pleased to give you.' And al-Shafi'i said: The slaba belongs to the killer if he is the one who killed him and he killed him while he was facing him; because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever kills a slain person, he has a right to his slaba.' And because he did more fighting, so he is singled out to show the distinction between him and others, and Ahmad said the same. And our proof is what we narrated, and because it was taken by the strength of the army of Islam, so it is booty of Islam, so it must be divided as booty. And what he narrated could be interpreted as nafal (award), so it is interpreted that way to reconcile between the two hadiths. And in what he narrated there is no condition of killing while facing. It is his mount, i.e., the mount of the slain person and what is on it of saddle and gear. I.e., included in the slaba is his mount with the saddle, his clothes, his weapon, and what is with him on the riding animal, and what is beyond that is not slaba. Likewise, what was with his servant on another riding animal, and likewise his slave and what is in his house.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses the ruling on slaba (the personal effects of a slain enemy combatant). The text explains the Hanafi position: if the imam does not designate the slaba for the killer as an incentive (nafal), then the slaba is part of the general booty to be divided among all participants equally. The Hanafi proof is the hadith reported by Habib ibn Abi Salama where the Prophet said the killer has no right to the slaba except what the imam gives him. The author then presents the view of Imam al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, who hold that the slaba belongs to the killer as a right, based on the hadith 'Whoever kills a slain person, he has a right to his slaba.' The Hanafi response is that the Shafi'i hadith can be interpreted as referring to nafal (an additional award given by the imam), thus reconciling both hadiths. The text also specifies what constitutes slaba: the slain person's mount with saddle, clothes, weapon, and items on the animal. Items not on the animal (e.g., with a servant on another animal, a slave, or items in the house) are not slaba.

Key Terms
سلب (salab): The personal effects of a slain enemy combatant, including mount, clothes, weapon, and items on the animal.
نفل (nafal): An additional award given by the imam to a warrior beyond the regular share of booty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does the killer of an enemy combatant receive the slaba?
A) Always, regardless of the imam's decision
B) Only if he killed the enemy while facing him
C) Only if the imam awards it as nafal
D) Only if he is the first to reach the slain enemy

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the slaba of a slain enemy is booty for the whole army unless the imam awards it to the killer as nafal.

Do Disbelievers Own Captured Property? The Hanafi Position and the Condition of Ihraz
Ruling on ownership of captured property by disbelievers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on when disbelievers own property they capture from other disbelievers or from Muslims, and explain why ihraz (taking to their abode) is the key condition.

Core Idea
Disbelievers own captured property only after ihraz; before that, ownership does not transfer.

This passage addresses a critical issue in the law of jihad: when does property taken by disbelievers become theirs? The Hanafi school distinguishes between mere capture and actual possession in their own territory. We will explore the conditions and the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
The passage discusses two scenarios: disbelievers capturing property from other disbelievers (e.g., Turks capturing Romans) and disbelievers capturing property from Muslims.
Both are treated similarly in Hanafi law, but the Shafi'i dispute focuses on Muslim property.
2
Understand ihraz as the condition
Ownership is established only after the captors take the property to their abode (dar al-harb). Before ihraz, the property can be recovered without compensation.
Ihraz is the act of bringing the captured property into the captors' territory.
3
Grasp the Hanafi rationale
The property of disbelievers is originally mubah (permissible) to other disbelievers. For Muslim property, inviolability (ismah) is conditional on the owner's ability to benefit; after ihraz, that ability is lost, so it becomes mubah in their regard.
The Hanafi position rests on the principle that ownership is tied to the capacity to control.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Imam Muhammad reported in al-Nawadir that disbelievers never own captured property, but the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah) is that they do own after ihraz.
The text indicates the author's apparent meaning supports ownership.
5
Contrast with Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that disbelievers never own Muslim property because it is inviolable and prohibited as an object of ownership. The Hanafi response is that inviolability is a legal ruling not addressed to disbelievers.
The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, captured Muslim property can become booty for the Muslim army if recovered after ihraz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أملاكهم الكفار. والمعنى باب استيلاء الكفار الإضافة من قبيل إضافة المصدر للفاعل أو من إضافة المصدر للفاعل والمفعول أن يستولي بعضهم على بعض والاستيلاء عبارة عن الاقتدار على المحل حالاً بالانتفاع بالمال، ومآلاً بالادخار إلى الزمان الثاني. سبى الترك الروم أي نصارى الروم، وهو جمع رومي. الترك جمع تركي، وما يفرق بينه وبين واحده بالتاء أو الياء فيه ثلاثة أقوال : قيل: إنه جمع، وقيل: إنه اسم جمع، والمختار مباح وهو سبب الملك. ملكوها لأن أموالهم مباحة والاستيلاء على المباح سبب الملك. وملكناه ما نجده إلخ: أي إن غلبنا نحن المسلمون على الترك بعد ما سبوا من الروم، وأخذوا أموالهم وأحرزوها بدارهم ملكنا، وحل لنا ما نجده السبايا والأموال من الروم؛ لأنها التحقت بالأموال الأصلية للترك، فكما نملك على الترك سائر أموالهم الأصلية، كذا نملك ما التحق بها من سبايا الروم وأموالهم. من ذلك: أي من الذي سباه الترك من الروم أو أخذوه من أموالهم. وأحرزوها بدارهم إلخ: لأن العصمة من الأحكام الشرعية وهم لم يخاطبوا بها فبقي في حقهم أموالنا مالاً غير معصوم فيملكونه، وقيد المسألة بالإحراز ؛ لأنه قبل الإحراز بدار الحرب لم بغير شيء، وإن وجدت بعد القسمة فهو لك بالقيمة"، ولأن يد المالك القديم زالت بغير رضاه، فكان له الأخذ بغير شيء نظراً له.
Chapter on the takeover of the disbelievers, i.e., explaining its rulings. The Turks took the Romans captive and took their property; they own it, and we own what we find of that if we overcome them. And if they overcome our property and take it to their abode, they own it; then if we overcome them. Considering their other properties of the disbelievers. And the meaning: Chapter on the takeover of the disbelievers; the idafa is of the type of adding the source to the agent or adding the source to the agent and object, that some take over others, and takeover means ability over the place currently by benefiting from the property, and ultimately by storing for the second time. The Turks took the Romans captive, i.e., the Christian Romans, and it is plural of Rumi. Turk is plural of Turki; there are three opinions on distinguishing between it and its singular with ta or ya: some say it is a plural, some say it is a collective noun, and the chosen view is that it is a generic noun; the specification of Turks and Romans is incidental because what is meant is the disbelievers of two lands. i.e., if the disbelievers of Turk capture the disbelievers of Rome and take their property, they own them. And in it is an indication that the harbi owns the harbi by force absolutely, whether he believes that or not. And from Muhammad in al-Nawadir: He does not own it at all. And it is said: He only owns it if he sees that and believes it. And the apparent from the author's statement is that ownership is established for them by their takeover of some over others before ihraz (taking possession), because of the existence of takeover of permissible property, which is a cause of ownership. They own it because their properties are permissible and takeover of permissible property is a cause of ownership. And we own what we find, etc.: i.e., if we Muslims overcome the Turks after they have captured the Romans and taken their properties and taken them to their abode, we own them, and what we find of the captives and properties from the Romans become permissible for us, because they have become attached to the original properties of the Turks, just as we own the other original properties of the Turks, so likewise we own what is attached to them of the Roman captives and properties. From that: i.e., from what the Turks captured from the Romans or took of their properties. And they took them to their abode, etc.: Because inviolability is from the legal rulings and they have not been addressed with them, so in their regard our property remains non-inviolable, so they own it. And the issue is conditioned on ihraz, because before ihraz in the abode of war, they have not owned it, so if the Muslims overcome them before ihraz and recover the properties, they belong to their owner without anything. And al-Shafi'i said: They do not own them, because these items are prohibited initially and finally because the takeover involves inviolable property, and what is prohibited cannot be a cause for ownership. And our proof is that the inviolability of a Muslim's property is due to his ability to benefit from it, and after ihraz in their abode his ability is gone, and the original principle regarding properties is permissibility, so they own them. Then if we overcome, etc.: i.e., if we conquer the abode of war after they have taken our properties and taken them to their abode, then the Muslim owners find their properties before the imam divides the booty among the Muslims, then they are for them without anything; and if they find them after division, they take them at value; because of his saying, peace be upon him, in the narration of Ibn Abbas: 'If you find it before division, it is yours without anything; if you find it after division, it is yours at value.' And because the hand of the original owner was removed without his consent, so he has the right to take without anything as a consideration for him.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on the takeover of the disbelievers. It discusses the ruling when one group of disbelievers (e.g., Turks) captures another group (e.g., Romans) and takes their property. According to the Hanafi school, the capturing disbelievers own that property because the property of disbelievers is not inviolable to other disbelievers; it is permissible for them. There is a narration from Imam Muhammad in al-Nawadir that they do not own it, but the relied-upon position is that they do. The condition is that they have taken the property to their abode (ihraz); before that, if Muslims recover it, it goes back to its original owner. If Muslims later capture the Turks after they have taken Roman property, the Muslims own that property because it becomes attached to the Turks' property. There is a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that disbelievers do not own Muslims' property at all because it is inviolable. The Hanafi response is that the inviolability is based on the Muslim's ability to benefit, and after the property is taken to dar al-harb, that ability is gone. The passage also addresses the situation where Muslims recover their own property from disbelievers: if found before the imam's division, the original owner takes it for free; if after division, he takes it at value, based on a hadith.

Key Terms
إحراز (ihraz): The act of taking captured property into the abode of the captors, which establishes ownership.
استيلاء (istila'): Takeover or gaining control over property or persons by force.
عصمة (ismah): Inviolability; legal protection of property or life.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when do disbelievers own property they capture from Muslims?
A) Immediately upon capture, because the Muslim loses control.
B) Only after they take the property to their abode (ihraz).
C) Never, because Muslim property is inviolable.
D) Only if the property was originally taken from other disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
Ownership of captured property by disbelievers requires ihraz; before that, Muslims can recover it for free.

6 min
Recovering Muslim Property from Disbelievers: Rules Before and After Division
Ruling on recovering captured property from disbelievers after ihraz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a Muslim who finds his captured property in the hands of disbelievers can reclaim it for free before the imam divides the booty, but must pay its value after division.

Core Idea
Recovery of captured property depends on timing: free before division, at value after division.

This lesson explains what happens when Muslims recover property that was taken by disbelievers after ihraz. The Hanafi school, based on a hadith, distinguishes between two situations: before the imam has divided the spoils, and after. This protects both the original owner and the recipients of the booty.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
The scenario: disbelievers have captured Muslim property and taken it to their abode (ihraz). Later, Muslims conquer their territory and find the property.
We assume ownership by disbelievers after ihraz per the Hanafi position from the previous lesson.
2
Identify the two sub-scenarios
The property may be found either before the imam has divided the spoils among the Muslim army, or after division.
The timing of division is determined by the imam's action.
3
Recall the hadith of Ibn Abbas
The Prophet ﷺ said: 'If you find it before division, it is yours without anything; if you find it after division, it is yours at value.' This is the direct proof.
The hadith is quoted in the passage.
4
Understand the rationale
The original owner's hand was removed without his consent, so he has a strong claim. Before division, the property has not yet been assigned to anyone, so returning it to the owner is fair. After division, the property has become the property of a new owner who acquired it through legitimate spoil, so the original owner must compensate to avoid injustice.
This balances the rights of the original owner and the new owner.

أملاكهم الكفار. والمعنى باب استيلاء الكفار الإضافة من قبيل إضافة المصدر للفاعل أو من إضافة المصدر للفاعل والمفعول أن يستولي بعضهم على بعض والاستيلاء عبارة عن الاقتدار على المحل حالاً بالانتفاع بالمال، ومآلاً بالادخار إلى الزمان الثاني. سبى الترك الروم أي نصارى الروم، وهو جمع رومي. الترك جمع تركي، وما يفرق بينه وبين واحده بالتاء أو الياء فيه ثلاثة أقوال : قيل: إنه جمع، وقيل: إنه اسم جمع، والمختار أنه اسم جنس، والتقييد بالترك والروم اتفاقي؛ لأن المراد بهما الكفار من بلدين، أي إذا سبى كفار الترك كفار الروم، وأخذوا أموالهم، يملكونها، وفيه إشارة إلى أن الحربي يملك الحربي بالقهر. مطلقاً . سواء كان مباح وهو سبب الملك. ملكوها لأن أموالهم مباحة والاستيلاء على المباح سبب الملك. وملكناه ما نجده إلخ: أي إن غلبنا نحن المسلمون على الترك بعد ما سبوا من الروم، وأخذوا أموالهم وأحرزوها بدارهم ملكنا، وحل لنا ما نجده السبايا والأموال من الروم؛ لأنها التحقت بالأموال الأصلية للترك، فكما نملك على الترك سائر أموالهم الأصلية، كذا نملك ما التحق بها من سبايا الروم وأموالهم. من ذلك: أي من الذي سباه الترك من الروم أو أخذوه من أموالهم. وأحرزوها بدارهم إلخ: لأن العصمة من الأحكام الشرعية وهم لم يخاطبوا بها فبقي في حقهم أموالنا مالاً غير معصوم فيملكونه، وقيد المسألة بالإحراز ؛ لأنه قبل الإحراز بدار الحرب لم يملكوها، حتى لو ظهر المسلمون عليهم قبل الإحراز، فاستردوا الأموال، فإنها تكون لمالكها بلا شيء، وقال الشافعي لا يملكونها؛ لأن هذه الأشياء محظورة ابتداء وانتهاء لورود الاستيلاء على مال معصوم، والمحظور لا يصلح سبباً للملك، ولنا أن عصمة مال المسلم لتمكنه من الانتفاع به، وبعد الإحراز بدارهم زال تمكنه والأصل في الأموال الإباحة فيملكونها. فإن غلبنا إلخ: أي فإن ظهرنا على دار أهل الحرب بعد ما أخذوا أموالنا وأحرزوها بدارهم، فوجد المسلمون المالكون أموالهم قبل قسمة الإمام الغنيمة بين المسلمين، فهي لهم بغير شيء، وإن وجدوها بعد القسمة، أخذوها بالقيمة؛ لقوله عليه في رواية ابن عباس: "إن وجدت قبل القسمة، فهو لك بغير شيء، وإن وجدت بعد القسمة فهو لك بالقيمة"، ولأن يد المالك القديم زالت بغير رضاه، فكان له الأخذ بغير شيء نظراً له.
Chapter on the takeover of the disbelievers, i.e., explaining its rulings. The Turks took the Romans captive and took their property; they own it, and we own what we find of that if we overcome them. And if they overcome our property and take it to their abode, they own it; then if we overcome them. Considering their other properties of the disbelievers. And the meaning: Chapter on the takeover of the disbelievers; the idafa is of the type of adding the source to the agent or adding the source to the agent and object, that some take over others, and takeover means ability over the place currently by benefiting from the property, and ultimately by storing for the second time. The Turks took the Romans captive, i.e., the Christian Romans, and it is plural of Rumi. Turk is plural of Turki; there are three opinions on distinguishing between it and its singular with ta or ya: some say it is a plural, some say it is a collective noun, and the chosen view is that it is a generic noun; the specification of Turks and Romans is incidental because what is meant is the disbelievers of two lands. i.e., if the disbelievers of Turk capture the disbelievers of Rome and take their property, they own them. And in it is an indication that the harbi owns the harbi by force absolutely, whether he believes that or not. And from Muhammad in al-Nawadir: He does not own it at all. And it is said: He only owns it if he sees that and believes it. And the apparent from the author's statement is that ownership is established for them by their takeover of some over others before ihraz (taking possession), because of the existence of takeover of permissible property, which is a cause of ownership. They own it because their properties are permissible and takeover of permissible property is a cause of ownership. And we own what we find, etc.: i.e., if we Muslims overcome the Turks after they have captured the Romans and taken their properties and taken them to their abode, we own them, and what we find of the captives and properties from the Romans become permissible for us, because they have become attached to the original properties of the Turks, just as we own the other original properties of the Turks, so likewise we own what is attached to them of the Roman captives and properties. From that: i.e., from what the Turks captured from the Romans or took of their properties. And they took them to their abode, etc.: Because inviolability is from the legal rulings and they have not been addressed with them, so in their regard our property remains non-inviolable, so they own it. And the issue is conditioned on ihraz, because before ihraz in the abode of war, they have not owned it, so if the Muslims overcome them before ihraz and recover the properties, they belong to their owner without anything. And al-Shafi'i said: They do not own them, because these items are prohibited initially and finally because the takeover involves inviolable property, and what is prohibited cannot be a cause for ownership. And our proof is that the inviolability of a Muslim's property is due to his ability to benefit from it, and after ihraz in their abode his ability is gone, and the original principle regarding properties is permissibility, so they own them. Then if we overcome, etc.: i.e., if we conquer the abode of war after they have taken our properties and taken them to their abode, then the Muslim owners find their properties before the imam divides the booty among the Muslims, then they are for them without anything; and if they find them after division, they take them at value; because of his saying, peace be upon him, in the narration of Ibn Abbas: 'If you find it before division, it is yours without anything; if you find it after division, it is yours at value.' And because the hand of the original owner was removed without his consent, so he has the right to take without anything as a consideration for him.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on the takeover of the disbelievers. It discusses the ruling when one group of disbelievers (e.g., Turks) captures another group (e.g., Romans) and takes their property. According to the Hanafi school, the capturing disbelievers own that property because the property of disbelievers is not inviolable to other disbelievers; it is permissible for them. There is a narration from Imam Muhammad in al-Nawadir that they do not own it, but the relied-upon position is that they do. The condition is that they have taken the property to their abode (ihraz); before that, if Muslims recover it, it goes back to its original owner. If Muslims later capture the Turks after they have taken Roman property, the Muslims own that property because it becomes attached to the Turks' property. There is a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that disbelievers do not own Muslims' property at all because it is inviolable. The Hanafi response is that the inviolability is based on the Muslim's ability to benefit, and after the property is taken to dar al-harb, that ability is gone. The passage also addresses the situation where Muslims recover their own property from disbelievers: if found before the imam's division, the original owner takes it for free; if after division, he takes it at value, based on a hadith.

Key Terms
قسمة (qismah): The division of booty by the imam among the Muslim soldiers.
قيمة (qimah): The market value of the property at the time of recovery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under the Hanafi school, if a Muslim's property is found in the hands of a soldier after the imam has divided the spoils, what must the original owner do to reclaim it?
A) He can take it for free because he is the original owner.
B) He must pay the soldier the value of the property.
C) He must get permission from the imam.
D) He cannot reclaim it because the property now belongs to the soldier.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule balances original ownership and booty distribution: recovery is free before division, but requires payment after.

Ownership by Disbelievers: The Principle of Istīlā’ and Iḥrāz
Foundation of ownership by kuffar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when disbelievers own property captured from Muslims and apply the condition of iḥrāz.

Core Idea
Disbelievers own captured Muslim property only after taking it into dar al-harb (iḥrāz).

This lesson explores the foundational principle of property ownership by non-Muslims over seized assets. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on whether the property is from other disbelievers or from Muslims, with the critical condition of iḥrāz (transport to dar al-harb). We will also examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and the Shafi'i objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actors and property
Consider the example: Turks capture Romans and take their property. According to Hanafi, the Turks own it because they took it to dar al-harb.
2
Identify the condition for ownership
The key condition is iḥrāz: physically taking the property into dar al-harb. Without iḥrāz, ownership does not transfer.
This applies whether the original owner is Muslim or non-Muslim.
3
Compare with Muslim property
If kuffar capture Muslim property and perform iḥrāz, they also own it. The ruling is the same.
The inviolability of Muslim property ceases with iḥrāz because the owner loses ability to benefit.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad holds that kuffar do not own each other's property at all (nawadir view). The relied-upon position is that they do.
This ikhtilaf does not affect the rule for Muslim property.
5
Understand the Shafi'i opposition
Shafi'i school argues that kuffar can never own Muslim property because it remains inviolable. Hanafi response: inviolability is conditional on ability to benefit.
This leads to different practical outcomes in recovery scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب استيلاء الكفار أي في بيان أحكامه. سبى الترك الروم، وأخذوا أموالهم ملكوها وملكنا ما نجده من ذلك إن غلبنا عليهم، وإن غلبوا على أموالنا وأحرزوها بدارهم ملكوها، فإن غلبنا عليهم، فمن وجد ملكه قبل القسمة أخذه مجاناً، وبعدها بالقيمة، وبالثمن لو اشتراه تاجر منهم وإن فقئ عينه وأخذ أرشه، فإن تكرر الأسر والشراء، أخذ الأول من الثاني بثمنه، ثم القديم بالثمنين.
Chapter on takeover by disbelievers: i.e., explaining its rulings. If the Turks capture the Romans and take their property, they own it; and we own what we find of that if we overcome them. And if they overcome our property and take it into their abode (dar al-harb), they own it. Then if we overcome them, whoever finds his property before division takes it for free; after division, he takes it at value; and if a merchant bought it from them, he takes it at the price. And if an eye was gouged out and he took its compensation, then if capture and purchase are repeated, the first purchaser takes it from the second at his price, then the original owner takes it from the first at both prices.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This is a commentary on the chapter of takeover by disbelievers (istila' al-kuffar) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The matn presents two main rulings: first, when non-Muslims (exemplified by Turks and Romans) capture each other's property, they own it by virtue of conquest, and if Muslims subsequently overcome them, they own that property as booty. Second, when disbelievers capture Muslim property and take it into dar al-harb, they own it due to lack of inviolability. The commentary expands on these points, noting that the condition of ihraz (taking into dar al-harb) is critical—without it, ownership does not transfer. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad in al-Nawadir holds that the kuffar do not own each other's property at all, while the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah) is that they do. The Shafi'i school disagrees entirely, arguing that kuffar cannot own Muslim property because it remains inviolable. The Hanafi response is that inviolability depends on the owner's ability to benefit, which is lost after ihraz. The second ruling elaborates on recovery: if a Muslim finds his property before the imam divides the booty, he takes it for free; after division, he pays its value. If a Muslim merchant bought it from the kuffar and brought it to dar al-Islam, the original owner may reclaim it by paying the price. If the merchant incurred an injury to the slave (e.g., gouging an eye) and received compensation, the owner still pays the full price. In cases of multiple captures and sales, the first purchaser recovers from the second at the price he paid, and the original owner recovers from the first at the sum of both prices. The hadith of Ibn Abbas is cited as proof for the before/after division distinction.

Key Terms
استيلاء (istīlā’): Overpowering and gaining control over something, specifically property or persons, by force.
إحراز (iḥrāz): Taking into one's own territory (dar al-harb) such that the original owner loses ability to benefit.
قسمة (qismah): Division of booty (ghanīmah) by the imam among Muslims after a battle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, when do disbelievers own property captured from Muslims?
A) Immediately upon capture
B) After taking it into dar al-harb
C) Only if it was originally their own property
D) They never own Muslim property

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that disbelievers own captured Muslim property only after ihraz, based on the cessation of inviolability.

Recovery of Property After Muslim Victory
Detailed recovery rules after Muslim victory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules for recovering property from booty or from a merchant, distinguishing before and after division.

Core Idea
Before division, recovery is free; after division, the original owner pays the value.

When Muslims recover property that had been in the hands of disbelievers, the original owner's right to reclaim it depends on timing. This lesson details the rules: free recovery before booty division, payment of value after division, and additional cases when a merchant bought the property or when injuries occurred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scenarios
The text distinguishes between finding property before division of booty (qabl al-qismah) and after division (ba'dahā).
Division is done by the imam after the battle.
2
Apply the rule for before division
If found before division, the original owner takes it for free (majjānan).
The property is still collectively owned; the original owner's right supersedes.
3
Apply the rule for after division
If found after division, the original owner pays its value (bil-qīmah) to the soldier who received it in division.
This compensates the soldier for his share.
4
Handle the merchant purchase case
If a Muslim merchant bought the property from the disbelievers, the original owner reclaims it by paying the price (bil-thaman) the merchant paid.
If the merchant incurred an injury (like gouging an eye) and received compensation, it does not reduce the price.
5
Deal with multiple captures and sales
If the property is captured and sold multiple times, the first purchaser takes it from the second at his price, then the original owner takes it from the first at the sum of both prices.
This ensures each party recovers their investment.

Source Text

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Jihad — Armed Conflict Rules

Enemy Ownership of Quasi-Free Muslims
Dispute over enemy ownership of quasi-free Muslims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on why warring disbelievers cannot own mudabbars, umm walads, and mukatabs, and explain the Maliki/Ahmad counter-view and the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Partial freedom prevents ownership: the enemy cannot own what is not wholly property.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq establishes a key principle: persons with any degree of freedom are immune from enemy ownership. The Hanafi school extends this to mudabbars, umm walads, and mukatabs, while Malik and Ahmad disagree. Let us explore the legal reasoning behind this dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
List the four categories of persons the enemy cannot own according to the text: free persons, mudabbars, umm walads, and mukatabs.
Note that all share some degree of freedom or inviolability.
2
Understand the illa
The text says 'because nothing yields its ruling except in its place, so the cause of ownership also requires a permissible place (mahall mubah).' This means that ownership can only arise over something that is permissible as property.
Partial freedom (hurriyah min wajh) makes them unsuitable as property.
3
Examine the opposing view
Malik and Ahmad analogize mudabbar and mukatab to regular slaves: seizure establishes ownership, but the master may redeem them for a price.
They do not extend this to free persons or umm walad.
4
Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the cause of ownership (seizure) requires a permissible locus, which is absent due to partial freedom. Thus, the enemy cannot own them.
The master reclaims them without payment.
5
Practical consequence (thamarah)
If Hanafi position is followed, the master reclaims for free; if Maliki, the master pays a price.
This difference has real-world implications for ransom or return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استيلاء الكفار وأحكامه ولم يملكوا حرنا ومدبّرنا وأم ولدنا ومكاتبنا ونملك عليهم جميع ذلك. وإن ندّ أي الكفار بالغلبة إليهم جمل، فأخذوه ملكوه إلى الكفار المراد به الدابة الجمل لتحقق الاستيلاء عليه أي نحن المسلمون بالغلبة أي على الكفار أي هرب وشرد سبب ولم يملكوا حرنا إلخ : أي لا يملك أهل الحرب بالغلبة علينا أحرارنا ومدبرينا وأمهات أولادنا ومكاتبينا؛ لأن كل شيء لا يفيد حكمه إلا في محله، فسبب الملك أيضاً يقتضي محلاً مباحاً، والحر معصوم لنفسه، وكذا سائر المذكورين؛ لأن الحرية قد ثبت فيهم من وجه فهؤلاء ليسوا بمال، وقال مالك وأحمد: يملك مدبرنا ومكاتبنا، ويأخذها السيد بالقيمة، وعن مالك: يفدي الإمام أم الولد وإلا يأخذها سيدها بالقيمة، ولا يدعها في أيديهم. وقوله : "ونملك عليهم جميع ذلك" يعني إن ظهرنا عليهم نملك رقابهم من الحر والمدبر وأم الولد والمكاتب؛ لأن الشرع أسقط عصمتهم جزاء على كفرهم؛ لأنهم استنكفوا أن يكونوا عباد فجعلهم. الله تعالى عبيد عبيده، وهذه الجناية منتفية عن مدبرينا ومكاتبينا، فإن قلت هذه الجناية منتفية عنهم إن كانوا مسلمين، وإن كانوا كفاراً فليست منتفية عنهم قلت على تقدير كفرهم ذاقوا وبال كفرهم لصيرورتهم عبيدنا. (مستخلص، عيني) ومكاتبنا: الحريتهم من وجه فيأخذ المالك مجاناً ويملكون القن والقنة ولو مسلمين. (ط) الله جميع ذلك: من الحر والمدبر وأم الولد والمكاتب لهم لعدم العصمة. (ط، عيني) ولو أبق إليهم قن لا، فلو أبق بفرس ومتاع، فاشترى رجل منهم كله ولو أبق إليهم قن لا، فلو أبق بفرس ومتاع، فاشترى رجل منهم كله مجاناً، وغيره بالثمن. وإن ابتاع مستأمن عبداً مؤمناً وأدخله دارهم أي بغير شيء لأنهم ملكوه عند أبي حنيفة أي اشترى أي حربي مستأمن في دارنا أو ذميا أخذ العبد أي من الكفار أي المالك قن لا [ أي رقيق مسلم فأخذوه قهرا (ط عيني)] أي لا يملكونه عند أبي حنيفة ولو أبق بعد ارتداده يملكونه بالأخذ اتفاقاً. (ط، عيني) فلو أبق بفرس إلخ هذا تفريع على قوله : ولو أبق عبد لا يملكونه أي إذا أبق عبد إليهم ومعه فرس ومتاع، فأخذ أهل الحرب ذلك كله ثم اشترى ذلك كله تاجر وأخرجه إلينا، فإن المولى يأخذ العبد بغير شيء عند أبي حنيفة، والفرس والمتاع بثمن اتفاقاً، وقالا : يأخذ العبد أيضاً بالثمن إن شاء، بناء على ما مر من أنهم يملكونه عندهما خلافاً له. فإن قيل كان ينبغي على قوله أن يأخذ الكل مجاناً؛ لأن العبد لما ظهرت يده على نفسه ظهرت على ما في يده فتمتنع ظهور يد الكافر، وأجيب بأن غايته أنه صار له يد بلا ملك؛ لأن الرق ينافيه فيملك الكفار ما يده بالاستيلاء، حاصل الجواب أنه ظهرت يد العبد على نفسه مع المنافي، وهو الرق، فكانت الظاهرة من وجه دون وجه فجعلناها ظاهرة في حق نفسه غير ظاهرة في حق المال. (مستخلص، فتح) منهم: أي كل ما ذكرنا من العبد والفرس والمتاع. (عيني) وغيره أي أخذ غير العبد وهو الفرس والمتاع. (عيني) كله ابتاع مستأمن إلخ هنا ثلاث مسائل، والجواب عن الكل قوله : "عتق"، الأولى دخل الحربي دارنا بأمان، فاشترى عبداً مسلماً وأدخله دار الحرب، عتق عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا لا يعتق؛ لأن إزالة ملك المستأمن عن العبد كانت مستحقة بطريق معين وهو البيع، فإذا انقطعت ولاية الجبر على البيع بدخوله في دار الحرب، بقي في يده عبداً على ما كان، ولأبي حنيفة أن تخليص المسلم عن ذل الكافر واجب فما كان في دار الإسلام يزول بالعوض، وهو الإزالة بالبيع؛ لأن مال المستأمن معصوم ما دام في دار الإسلام، فإذا دخل في دار الحرب، انتهت العصمة بانتهاء الأمان، وعجز القاضي عن إخراجه عن ملكه فاستحق الإزالة بالعتق، فيعتق. فإن قيل: الإحراز بدار الحرب سبب لإثبات الملك فيما إذا لم يكن ملكاً له كما إذا أسروا عبداً فأدخلوه دارهم ملكوه فيستحيل أن يزول ملكه بالإحراز؛ لأن الإحراز لما صار سبباً لإثبات الملك ابتداء فأولى أن يبقى ملكهم الثابت بالشراء، وإلا يلزم أن يكون ما هو مثبت للملك مزيلاً له وهو الإحراز، قلنا: ليس هذا كأسرهم عبداً من دارنا؛ لأنهم لا يملكون بالأخذ حتى يستحق عليهم الإزالة بسبب الاستيلاء، وإنما يملكونها بالإحراز بخلاف ما نحن فيه؛ لأن المستأمن ملكه بالشراء، فاستحق عليه الإزالة بالبيع ما دام في دارنا فإذا تعذر فبالعتق عنده، ثم التقييد في العبد بالمؤمن اتفاقي؛ لأن الحكم لا يختلف في العبد الذمي. (مستخلص، فتح) أدخله دارهم: أي أدخل المستأمن العبد المؤمن دار الحرب. (معدن)
Enemy seizure and its rulings: They do not own our free person, mudabbar, umm walad, and mukatab, and we own all of that over them. And if a camel flees to the disbelievers, they take it and own it... The meaning of 'they do not own our free person, etc.': The warring people do not own, by victory over us, our free persons, mudabbars, mothers of our children, and mukatabs, because nothing yields its ruling except in its place, so the cause of ownership also requires a permissible place, and the free person is inviolable for himself, and likewise the rest, because freedom is established in them from one aspect, so these are not property. Malik and Ahmad said: They own our mudabbar and mukatab, and the master takes them for a price. From Malik: The imam ransoms the umm walad, otherwise her master takes her for a price, and does not leave her in their hands. His statement: 'And we own all of that over them' means if we overcome them, we own their necks from free, mudabbar, umm walad, and mukatab; because the law removed their immunity as a penalty for their disbelief, because they were disdainful of being slaves, so Allah made them slaves of His slaves. This offense is absent regarding our mudabbars and mukatabs. If you say: This offense is absent if they are Muslims, but if they are disbelievers it is not absent. I say: On the assumption of their disbelief, they taste the evil of their disbelief by becoming our slaves. (Talkhis, 'Ayni) And our mukatab: their freedom from one aspect, so the master takes him for free, and they own the male and female slave even if Muslims. (T) Allah, all of that: from free, mudabbar, umm walad, and mukatab, for them due to lack of immunity. (T, 'Ayni) And if a male slave runs away to them, no; then if he runs away with a horse and goods, and a man from them buys all of it for free, and other than it for a price. And if a musta'min buys a believing slave and takes him into their abode... [continues]
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq is an expansion on the rulings regarding enemy seizure (istila' al-kuffar). The author presents multiple legal scenarios, each with its own ruling and often with intra- or inter-madhhab disputes. First, the general principle: warring disbelievers (ahl al-harb) cannot own free Muslims, mudabbars (slaves whose freedom is tied to their master's death), umm walads (slave mothers of a free child), or mukatabs (slaves under a contract of manumission). This is because these persons possess inviolability (isma) or partial freedom, making them unfit to be owned as property. Malik and Ahmad disagree regarding mudabbars and mukatabs, allowing ownership with the master's right to redeem. Abu Hanifa also holds that when Muslims overcome enemy forces, they own all categories of enemy persons, including their free, due to the removal of their immunity for disbelief. The passage then addresses runaway property. If a camel flees to the enemy and they take it, they own it because it is mere property with no self-ownership. For a runaway slave (abeq), Abu Hanifa rules that the enemy does not own him because the slave's own hand (yad) over himself becomes effective upon leaving the Islamic territory, making him inviolable; the Sahibayn hold that the enemy owns him through seizure (istila) like other property. Subcases involve a slave fleeing with a horse and goods: Abu Hanifa allows the master to reclaim the slave for free but the horse and goods for a price, while the Sahibayn require a price for all. Finally, if a musta'min (disbeliever granted safe passage) buys a Muslim slave and takes him to dar al-harb, Abu Hanifa rules the slave becomes free because the obligation to rescue a Muslim from humiliation cannot be fulfilled by forced sale, so emancipation becomes necessary. The Sahibayn hold the slave remains owned. Each ruling is supported by detailed reasoning (ta'lil) and addresses potential objections, reflecting the rigorous Hanafi method.

Key Terms
حر (ḥurr): A free person, not owned by anyone.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose manumission is conditional upon the master's death.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, thus acquiring freedom upon his death.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave under a contract of manumission for a fixed sum payable in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a Muslim's mukatab is captured by warring disbelievers, what is the ruling?
A) The enemy owns the mukatab; the master can redeem him for a price.
B) The enemy does not own the mukatab; the master reclaims him without payment.
C) The mukatab becomes free upon capture.
D) The ownership is shared between enemy and master.

Key Takeaway
Partial freedom (hurriyah min wajh) renders a person impermissible as property, so the enemy cannot own mudabbars, umm walads, or mukatabs.

Runaway Slaves and Musta'min: Intra-Hanafi Disputes
Intra-Hanafi dispute on enemy ownership of runaway slaves and musta'min's slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding enemy ownership of a runaway slave, and derive the ruling for the slave fleeing with property.

Core Idea
A runaway slave's own hand (yad) emerges, preventing enemy ownership according to Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn hold that the enemy owns him.

This passage presents internal Hanafi disputes on whether a runaway slave (abeq) becomes enemy property. Abu Hanifa's unique view is that the slave gains a valid hand over himself upon leaving dar al-Islam, making him inviolable. The Sahibayn analogize him to other property. This also affects the musta'min's slave scenario.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling on runaway slave
The text states: 'And if a male slave runs away to them, no (they do not own him).' Then it presents the sub-case of slave fleeing with horse and goods.
The word 'no' indicates the position of Abu Hanifa.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's dalil
Abu Hanifa argues that the slave's own yad over himself becomes effective upon leaving dar al-Islam, making him ma'sum (inviolable). The enemy cannot own him.
This is derived from the principle that ownership requires a permissible object; the slave is not such because of his inviolability.
3
Sahibayn's view and their dalil
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the enemy owns the runaway slave through istila', just like any other property. They base this on qiyas with other movable property.
They do not recognize the slave's hand as effective.
4
Sub-case: slave with horse and goods
If the slave flees with a horse and goods, Abu Hanifa distinguishes: the slave (due to his hand) is not owned, but the horse and goods are owned by the enemy.
The master reclaims the slave for free, but must pay a price for the horse and goods.
5
Musta'min's slave scenario
If a musta'min (disbeliever with aman) buys a Muslim slave and takes him to dar al-harb, Abu Hanifa says the slave becomes free; the Sahibayn say he remains owned.
Reasoning: obligation to rescue Muslim from humiliation, and the limitation of forced sale.

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq is an expansion on the rulings regarding enemy seizure (istila' al-kuffar). The author presents multiple legal scenarios, each with its own ruling and often with intra- or inter-madhhab disputes. First, the general principle: warring disbelievers (ahl al-harb) cannot own free Muslims, mudabbars (slaves whose freedom is tied to their master's death), umm walads (slave mothers of a free child), or mukatabs (slaves under a contract of manumission). This is because these persons possess inviolability (isma) or partial freedom, making them unfit to be owned as property. Malik and Ahmad disagree regarding mudabbars and mukatabs, allowing ownership with the master's right to redeem. Abu Hanifa also holds that when Muslims overcome enemy forces, they own all categories of enemy persons, including their free, due to the removal of their immunity for disbelief. The passage then addresses runaway property. If a camel flees to the enemy and they take it, they own it because it is mere property with no self-ownership. For a runaway slave (abeq), Abu Hanifa rules that the enemy does not own him because the slave's own hand (yad) over himself becomes effective upon leaving the Islamic territory, making him inviolable; the Sahibayn hold that the enemy owns him through seizure (istila) like other property. Subcases involve a slave fleeing with a horse and goods: Abu Hanifa allows the master to reclaim the slave for free but the horse and goods for a price, while the Sahibayn require a price for all. Finally, if a musta'min (disbeliever granted safe passage) buys a Muslim slave and takes him to dar al-harb, Abu Hanifa rules the slave becomes free because the obligation to rescue a Muslim from humiliation cannot be fulfilled by forced sale, so emancipation becomes necessary. The Sahibayn hold the slave remains owned. Each ruling is supported by detailed reasoning (ta'lil) and addresses potential objections, reflecting the rigorous Hanafi method.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A disbeliever granted safe passage (aman) into Muslim territory.
يد (yad): Hand; legal possession or control.
أبق (abaqa): To run away (of a slave).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), if a Muslim slave runs away to enemy territory, what is the ruling regarding his ownership?
A) The enemy does not own the slave; the master must pay a price.
B) The enemy owns the slave; the master may reclaim him for free.
C) The enemy owns the slave; the master may reclaim him by paying a price.
D) The slave becomes free upon entering enemy territory.

Key Takeaway
Within the Hanafi school, Abu Hanifa holds that a runaway slave is not owned by the enemy due to his own emerging hand, while the Sahibayn hold that he is owned, with practical differences in ransom.

The Fugitive Slave: Ownership Dispute Between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ownership of fugitive slave and property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's position on a slave fleeing with goods to enemy territory and explain the difference from the Sahibayn's view, including the underlying legal reasoning.

Core Idea
A slave's defective hand cannot extend ownership to accompanying goods.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the legal effect of a slave's flight to enemy territory with property. The central question is whether enemies acquire ownership of the slave and his goods upon seizure from the fugitive. Abu Hanifa distinguished between the slave himself and his property, while the two companions considered both as enemy spoils. The reasoning hinges on the nature of the slave's capacity to possess, a key principle in Hanafi law of persons.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa as given in the text. Note what the master can reclaim without payment and what he must pay for.
Master takes slave free, goods with price.
2
Identify the opposing view
State the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). How does their ruling differ from Abu Hanifa?
Sahibayn say master must pay for both.
3
Follow the legal reasoning
Analyze the key argument: the slave's 'hand' (control) over himself is defective due to slavery. Why does that lead to different treatment of his person vs. his property?
The slave's hand is compromised, so it does not prevent enemy ownership of goods.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The text presents an objection: if the slave's hand prevents enemy control over himself, why not over goods? Examine the Hanafi response.
Response: The slave's hand is only apparent over himself, not over his possessions.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference between the two rulings in terms of the master's cost to reclaim his slave and goods.
Abu Hanifa: slave free, goods paid. Sahibayn: both paid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أبق إليهم قن لا، فلو أبق بفرس ومتاع، فاشترى رجل منهم فأخذوهما مجاناً، وغيره بالثمن. هذا تفريع على قوله: ولو أبق عبد لا يملكونه أي إذا أبق عبد إليهم ومعه فرس ومتاع، فأخذ أهل الحرب ذلك كله ثم اشترى ذلك كله تاجر وأخرجه إلينا، فإن المولى يأخذ العبد بغير شيء عند أبي حنيفة، والفرس والمتاع بثمن اتفاقاً، وقالا: يأخذ العبد أيضاً بالثمن إن شاء، بناء على ما مر من أنهم يملكونه عندهما خلافاً له. فإن قيل: كان ينبغي على قوله أن يأخذ الكل مجاناً؛ لأن العبد لما ظهرت يده على نفسه ظهرت على ما في يده فتمتنع ظهور يد الكافر، وأجيب: بأن غايته أنه صار له يد بلا ملك؛ لأن الرق ينافيه فيملك الكفار ما يده بالاستيلاء، حاصل الجواب أنه ظهرت يد العبد على نفسه مع المنافي، وهو الرق، فكانت الظاهرة من وجه دون وجه فجعلناها ظاهرة في حق نفسه غير ظاهرة في حق المال.
And if a slave flees to them, they do not own him. So if he flees with a horse and goods, and a man buys from them, then they (the slave) are taken for free and the other (goods) for the price. This is a branch from his statement: 'And if a slave flees, they do not own him.' That is, if a slave flees to them with a horse and goods, and the people of war take all that, then a merchant buys all that and brings it out to us, then the master takes the slave without anything according to Abu Hanifa, and the horse and goods with a price by agreement. And the two companions said: He takes the slave also with a price if he wishes, based on what has passed that they own him according to them contrary to him. For it was said: According to his view, he should take all for free, because when the slave's hand appears over himself, it appears over what is in his hand, thus preventing the appearance of the disbeliever's hand. It was answered: Its utmost is that he obtains a hand without ownership, because slavery contradicts it, so the disbelievers own what is in his hand by seizure. The summary of the answer is that the slave's hand appeared over himself along with a contradiction, which is slavery, so it was apparent from one aspect but not another; thus we made it apparent with respect to himself but not apparent with respect to property.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the status of a slave who flees to enemy territory with goods. Abu Hanifa holds that the enemies do not own the slave by mere seizure, so if a merchant buys the slave and goods from the enemies and brings them back to Islamic territory, the original master may reclaim the slave without any payment, but must pay the price for the goods. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the enemies own both the slave and the goods, so the master may reclaim both by paying the price. The commentary then addresses an objection: if the slave's own 'hand' (control) over himself negates the enemy's control, then why isn't the goods also free? The answer is that the slave's hand over himself is defective due to his slavery, so it does not extend to his possessions. Thus the enemies' seizure of the goods is effective. This ruling is grounded in the principle that a slave cannot legally own property, so his possession is not true ownership.

Key Terms
أبق (abaqa): To flee from one's master to enemy territory.
قن (qinn): A male slave who is owned fully.
مولى (mawla): The master who owns the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the status of a slave who flees to enemy territory with goods, if a merchant buys both from the enemies and brings them back to Islamic territory?
A) The master takes both the slave and goods without paying anything.
B) The master takes the slave without payment, but pays the price for the goods.
C) The master must pay the price for both the slave and goods.
D) The master loses all rights; the merchant keeps both.

Key Takeaway
A fugitive slave's own control is defective due to slavery, so enemy seizure of his person is ineffective, but seizure of his goods is effective; Abu Hanifa's view is mufta bihi.

Musta'min Buying a Muslim Slave and Taking Him to Dar al-Harb
Ruling with proof: intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a Muslim slave bought by a musta'min and taken to dar al-harb, differentiate Abu Hanifa's position from the Sahibayn, and identify the practical consequence of the ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
A Muslim slave bought by a musta'min becomes free upon entry into dar al-harb because the duty to rescue overrides ownership.

This lesson examines the first of three cases from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: a musta'min purchases a Muslim slave and transports him to dar al-harb. The passage records a significant intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples. By unpacking the reasoning on both sides, you will understand how core principles – inviolability of property, obligation to rescue, and the effect of dar al-harb – interact to produce divergent rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
Determine who the musta'min is (a non-Muslim with aman), what he did (bought a Muslim slave), and the consequence (entry into dar al-harb).
2
State the dispute
Abu Hanifa says the slave becomes free immediately; the Sahibayn say he remains a slave. Write this clearly.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's evidence
They argue that removal of ownership was owed via a specific path (sale). Since the judge can no longer compel sale in dar al-harb, the musta'min's ownership persists unchanged.
Focus on the concept of 'path of removal' (tariq al-izalah).
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's evidence
He argues that rescuing a Muslim from humiliation (takhlees) is obligatory. The musta'min's aman ends in dar al-harb, so his property loses inviolability. Since sale is impossible, emancipation becomes the only means.
Note the shift from sale to freeing as the method of removal.
5
Evaluate the objection and response
An objection states that ihraz (bringing property into dar al-harb) normally confirms ownership; how could it destroy it? The response distinguishes ownership by capture (where ihraz establishes ownership) from ownership by purchase (which pre-exists).
This distinction is key to understanding the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ابتاع مستأمن عبداً مؤمناً وأدخله دارهم عتق. هنا ثلاث مسائل، والجواب عن الكل قوله: "عتق"، الأولى دخل الحربي دارنا بأمان، فاشترى عبداً مسلماً وأدخله دار الحرب، عتق عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا لا يعتق؛ لأن إزالة ملك المستأمن عن العبد كانت مستحقة بطريق معين وهو البيع، فإذا انقطعت ولاية الجبر على البيع بدخوله في دار الحرب، بقي في يده عبداً على ما كان، ولأبي حنيفة أن تخليص المسلم عن ذل الكافر واجب فما كان في دار الإسلام يزول بالعوض، وهو الإزالة بالبيع؛ لأن مال المستأمن معصوم ما دام في دار الإسلام، فإذا دخل في دار الحرب، انتهت العصمة بانتهاء الأمان، وعجز القاضي عن إخراجه عن ملكه فاستحق الإزالة بالعتق، فيعتق. فإن قيل: الإحراز بدار الحرب سبب لإثبات الملك فيما إذا لم يكن ملكاً له كما إذا أسروا عبداً فأدخلوه دارهم ملكوه فيستحيل أن يزول ملكه بالإحراز؛ لأن الإحراز لما صار سبباً لإثبات الملك ابتداء فأولى أن يبقى ملكهم الثابت بالشراء، وإلا يلزم أن يكون ما هو مثبت للملك مزيلاً له وهو الإحراز، قلنا: ليس هذا كأسرهم عبداً من دارنا؛ لأنهم لا يملكون بالأخذ حتى يستحق عليهم الإزالة بسبب الاستيلاء، وإنما يملكونها بالإحراز بخلاف ما نحن فيه؛ لأن المستأمن ملكه بالشراء، فاستحق عليه الإزالة بالبيع ما دام في دارنا فإذا تعذر فبالعتق عنده، ثم التقييد في العبد بالمؤمن اتفاقي؛ لأن الحكم لا يختلف في العبد الذمي.

أو أسلم عبد حربي ثم خرج فجاءنا، أو ظهرنا عليهم عتق. هذه مسألة ثانية أي إذا أسلم عبد الحربي في دار الحرب ثم خرج إلى دار الإسلام أو إلى عسكر المسلمين، فهو حر؛ لما روي عن ابن عباس لما قال: "أعتق رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم الطائف من خرج إليه من عبيد المشركين"، رواه أحمد أي من أسلم وخرج إليه فقضى بعتقه، وقال: "هم عتقاء الله تعالى"؛ ولأنه أحرز نفسه بالخروج إلينا مراغماً لمولاه، وقيد بقوله: "فجاءنا"؛ لأنه إن لم يخرج إلينا بعد الإسلام فهو عبد على حاله ولا يعتق. فجاءنا: أي العبد وخرج إلى دار الإسلام أو إلى عسكر المسلمين. أو ظهرنا عليهم: هذه مسألة ثالثة أي إذا أسلم عبد الحربي في دار الحرب، وغلبنا على أهل الحرب الذي هو عندهم عتق أيضاً؛ لما روينا، ولأنه التحق بمنعة المسلمين واعتبار يده أولى من اعتبار يد المسلمين؛ لأنها أسبق ثبوتاً على نفسه، والحاجة في حقه إلى زيادة التوكيل وفي حقهم إلى إثبات اليد ابتداء فلهذا كان اعتبار يده أولى. عتق أي العبد في الصور كلها بلا إعتاق ولا ولاء لا حد عليه.
A musta'min buys a believing slave and takes him into their abode: he is free. Here are three issues, and the answer to all is his statement: 'free'. The first: a harbi enters our abode with aman, then buys a Muslim slave and takes him to dar al-harb. He is free according to Abu Hanifa, and the two companions say he is not free, because the removal of the musta'min's ownership over the slave was owed via a specific path, which is sale. So when the authority of compulsion over sale is cut off by his entry into dar al-harb, it remains in his hand as a slave as before. And for Abu Hanifa: it is obligatory to rescue the Muslim from the humiliation of the disbeliever. So what was in dar al-Islam is removed by compensation, which is removal by sale, because the property of the musta'min is inviolable as long as he is in dar al-Islam. So when he enters dar al-harb, inviolability ends with the end of aman, and the judge is unable to remove it from his ownership, so removal by freeing becomes due, so it is freed. For it was said: The seizure (ihraz) in dar al-harb is a cause for establishing ownership if it was not already owned, like when they capture a slave and take him into their abode, they own him. So it is impossible that his ownership be removed by seizure, because when seizure became a cause for establishing ownership initially, it would be more appropriate that their ownership established by purchase remain, otherwise it would necessitate that what establishes ownership also removes it, i.e., seizure. We said: This is not like their capture of a slave from our abode, because they did not own by taking until removal becomes due upon them due to seizure; rather, they own by ihraz, contrary to our case. Because the musta'min owned by purchase, so removal by sale was due upon him as long as he was in our abode; when that becomes impossible, it is by freeing according to him. Then the restriction to a believing slave is agreed upon, because the ruling does not differ regarding a dhimmi slave.

Or a slave of a harbi becomes Muslim, then leaves and comes to us, or we overcome them: he is free. This is the second issue: i.e., when a slave of a harbi becomes Muslim in dar al-harb, then leaves to dar al-Islam or to the Muslim army, he is free. Because of what is narrated from Ibn Abbas when he said: 'The Messenger of God freed for Ta'if whoever came to him from the slaves of the polytheists.' Ahmad narrated it: i.e., whoever became Muslim and came to him, he ruled for his freedom, and said: 'They are the freed ones of God.' And because he secured himself by coming out to us, fleeing from his master. It is restricted by his statement: 'he comes to us', for if he does not come to us after Islam, he remains a slave as before and is not freed. 'He comes to us' means the slave leaves to dar al-Islam or to the Muslim army. 'Or we overcome them': this is the third issue: i.e., when a slave of a harbi becomes Muslim in dar al-harb, and we overcome the people of war with whom he is, he is also free. Because of what we narrated, and because he has joined the protection of the Muslims, and consideration of his hand is more deserving than consideration of the hand of the Muslims, because it is prior in establishment over himself, and his need is for additional agency, while their need is for initial establishment of the hand, so for this reason his hand is considered first. Free means the slave in all the cases without (need for) freeing or wala' and no hadd (punishment) upon him.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct cases (masa'il) from the Hanafi school, each concerning when a Muslim slave becomes free without formal emancipation. The first case: a musta'min (a non-Muslim given safe-conduct) buys a Muslim slave and takes him to dar al-harb. Abu Hanifa holds that the slave becomes free immediately upon entry, because the obligation to rescue a Muslim from humiliation overrides the musta'min's right of ownership. Since the musta'min's aman ends upon entering enemy territory, the judge can no longer compel him to sell the slave; thus the alternative of forced emancipation applies. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, holding that the musta'min's ownership continues because the normal means of removal (sale) is unavailable, not that the slave becomes free. The text answers an objection that ihraz (seizure) normally confirms ownership, so it should not destroy it; the rebuttal distinguishes between ownership by capture (which begins with seizure) and ownership by purchase (which pre-exists). The second case: a slave of a harbi (enemy combatant) embraces Islam while still in dar al-harb and then escapes to Muslim territory. He becomes free based on the hadith of Ibn Abbas about the Prophet freeing the slaves of Thaqif who came to him, and because he has secured his own freedom by fleeing. This is conditional on him actually reaching Muslim territory. The third case: if a slave of a harbi becomes Muslim in dar al-harb and then Muslims conquer that territory, he also becomes free, because his own hand (control over himself) is prior in establishment, and his need for agency outweighs the Muslims' need to establish control over him. In all three cases, the slave is free without any formal manumission, and no wala' (clientage) is established, nor any hadd punishment for any infraction.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim who enters Islamic territory with a guarantee of safety (aman).
إحراز (ihraz): The act of bringing captured property into one's own territory (dar al-harb), which establishes ownership.
عتقاء الله ('utaqa' Allah): Freed ones of God – referring to slaves who become free by divine decree through embracing Islam and escaping to Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does the slave become free when the musta'min takes him to dar al-harb?
A) Because the musta'min's ownership is nullified by the judge.
B) Because the aman ends and the judge can no longer compel sale, making emancipation obligatory.
C) Because the slave can now buy his own freedom.
D) Because the musta'min automatically emancipates him upon entry.

Key Takeaway
The duty to rescue a Muslim from kafir humiliation can override ownership rights when the normal legal remedy (sale) is no longer enforceable.
