Slave of Harbi Embracing Islam and Escaping to Muslims
Ruling with proof: case from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a slave of a harbi to become free by embracing Islam, and explain the proof from the hadith of Ibn Abbas.

Core Idea
A slave of a harbi becomes free only if he embraces Islam and physically escapes to Muslim territory.

This lesson addresses the second case from the passage: a slave belonging to an enemy combatant (harbi) who accepts Islam while still in dar al-harb and then flees to the Muslims. The text relies on a hadith of Ibn Abbas about the Prophet freeing the slaves of Ta'if who came to him. The key condition is the physical act of leaving dar al-harb and joining the Muslim community.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
The slave is owned by a harbi (enemy non-Muslim). He embraces Islam while still in dar al-harb. Then he leaves dar al-harb and comes to dar al-Islam or the Muslim army.
2
State the ruling
The slave becomes free immediately upon reaching the Muslims. No formal manumission is needed, and no wala' (clientage) is established for the former master.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
The hadith: The Prophet freed the slaves of Ta'if who came to him. The text also mentions the phrase 'they are the freed ones of God.'
This hadith is ahad, but it is used as a proof.
4
Identify the condition for freedom
The slave must actually come out to the Muslims. Simply embracing Islam while staying in dar al-harb does not free him.
The text explicitly says: 'if he does not come out to us after Islam, he remains a slave.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ابتاع مستأمن عبداً مؤمناً وأدخله دارهم عتق. هنا ثلاث مسائل، والجواب عن الكل قوله: "عتق"، الأولى دخل الحربي دارنا بأمان، فاشترى عبداً مسلماً وأدخله دار الحرب، عتق عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا لا يعتق؛ لأن إزالة ملك المستأمن عن العبد كانت مستحقة بطريق معين وهو البيع، فإذا انقطعت ولاية الجبر على البيع بدخوله في دار الحرب، بقي في يده عبداً على ما كان، ولأبي حنيفة أن تخليص المسلم عن ذل الكافر واجب فما كان في دار الإسلام يزول بالعوض، وهو الإزالة بالبيع؛ لأن مال المستأمن معصوم ما دام في دار الإسلام، فإذا دخل في دار الحرب، انتهت العصمة بانتهاء الأمان، وعجز القاضي عن إخراجه عن ملكه فاستحق الإزالة بالعتق، فيعتق. فإن قيل: الإحراز بدار الحرب سبب لإثبات الملك فيما إذا لم يكن ملكاً له كما إذا أسروا عبداً فأدخلوه دارهم ملكوه فيستحيل أن يزول ملكه بالإحراز؛ لأن الإحراز لما صار سبباً لإثبات الملك ابتداء فأولى أن يبقى ملكهم الثابت بالشراء، وإلا يلزم أن يكون ما هو مثبت للملك مزيلاً له وهو الإحراز، قلنا: ليس هذا كأسرهم عبداً من دارنا؛ لأنهم لا يملكون بالأخذ حتى يستحق عليهم الإزالة بسبب الاستيلاء، وإنما يملكونها بالإحراز بخلاف ما نحن فيه؛ لأن المستأمن ملكه بالشراء، فاستحق عليه الإزالة بالبيع ما دام في دارنا فإذا تعذر فبالعتق عنده، ثم التقييد في العبد بالمؤمن اتفاقي؛ لأن الحكم لا يختلف في العبد الذمي.

أو أسلم عبد حربي ثم خرج فجاءنا، أو ظهرنا عليهم عتق. هذه مسألة ثانية أي إذا أسلم عبد الحربي في دار الحرب ثم خرج إلى دار الإسلام أو إلى عسكر المسلمين، فهو حر؛ لما روي عن ابن عباس لما قال: "أعتق رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم الطائف من خرج إليه من عبيد المشركين"، رواه أحمد أي من أسلم وخرج إليه فقضى بعتقه، وقال: "هم عتقاء الله تعالى"؛ ولأنه أحرز نفسه بالخروج إلينا مراغماً لمولاه، وقيد بقوله: "فجاءنا"؛ لأنه إن لم يخرج إلينا بعد الإسلام فهو عبد على حاله ولا يعتق. فجاءنا: أي العبد وخرج إلى دار الإسلام أو إلى عسكر المسلمين. أو ظهرنا عليهم: هذه مسألة ثالثة أي إذا أسلم عبد الحربي في دار الحرب، وغلبنا على أهل الحرب الذي هو عندهم عتق أيضاً؛ لما روينا، ولأنه التحق بمنعة المسلمين واعتبار يده أولى من اعتبار يد المسلمين؛ لأنها أسبق ثبوتاً على نفسه، والحاجة في حقه إلى زيادة التوكيل وفي حقهم إلى إثبات اليد ابتداء فلهذا كان اعتبار يده أولى. عتق أي العبد في الصور كلها بلا إعتاق ولا ولاء لا حد عليه.
A musta'min buys a believing slave and takes him into their abode: he is free. Here are three issues, and the answer to all is his statement: 'free'. The first: a harbi enters our abode with aman, then buys a Muslim slave and takes him to dar al-harb. He is free according to Abu Hanifa, and the two companions say he is not free, because the removal of the musta'min's ownership over the slave was owed via a specific path, which is sale. So when the authority of compulsion over sale is cut off by his entry into dar al-harb, it remains in his hand as a slave as before. And for Abu Hanifa: it is obligatory to rescue the Muslim from the humiliation of the disbeliever. So what was in dar al-Islam is removed by compensation, which is removal by sale, because the property of the musta'min is inviolable as long as he is in dar al-Islam. So when he enters dar al-harb, inviolability ends with the end of aman, and the judge is unable to remove it from his ownership, so removal by freeing becomes due, so it is freed. For it was said: The seizure (ihraz) in dar al-harb is a cause for establishing ownership if it was not already owned, like when they capture a slave and take him into their abode, they own him. So it is impossible that his ownership be removed by seizure, because when seizure became a cause for establishing ownership initially, it would be more appropriate that their ownership established by purchase remain, otherwise it would necessitate that what establishes ownership also removes it, i.e., seizure. We said: This is not like their capture of a slave from our abode, because they did not own by taking until removal becomes due upon them due to seizure; rather, they own by ihraz, contrary to our case. Because the musta'min owned by purchase, so removal by sale was due upon him as long as he was in our abode; when that becomes impossible, it is by freeing according to him. Then the restriction to a believing slave is agreed upon, because the ruling does not differ regarding a dhimmi slave.

Or a slave of a harbi becomes Muslim, then leaves and comes to us, or we overcome them: he is free. This is the second issue: i.e., when a slave of a harbi becomes Muslim in dar al-harb, then leaves to dar al-Islam or to the Muslim army, he is free. Because of what is narrated from Ibn Abbas when he said: 'The Messenger of God freed for Ta'if whoever came to him from the slaves of the polytheists.' Ahmad narrated it: i.e., whoever became Muslim and came to him, he ruled for his freedom, and said: 'They are the freed ones of God.' And because he secured himself by coming out to us, fleeing from his master. It is restricted by his statement: 'he comes to us', for if he does not come to us after Islam, he remains a slave as before and is not freed. 'He comes to us' means the slave leaves to dar al-Islam or to the Muslim army. 'Or we overcome them': this is the third issue: i.e., when a slave of a harbi becomes Muslim in dar al-harb, and we overcome the people of war with whom he is, he is also free. Because of what we narrated, and because he has joined the protection of the Muslims, and consideration of his hand is more deserving than consideration of the hand of the Muslims, because it is prior in establishment over himself, and his need is for additional agency, while their need is for initial establishment of the hand, so for this reason his hand is considered first. Free means the slave in all the cases without (need for) freeing or wala' and no hadd (punishment) upon him.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct cases (masa'il) from the Hanafi school, each concerning when a Muslim slave becomes free without formal emancipation. The first case: a musta'min (a non-Muslim given safe-conduct) buys a Muslim slave and takes him to dar al-harb. Abu Hanifa holds that the slave becomes free immediately upon entry, because the obligation to rescue a Muslim from humiliation overrides the musta'min's right of ownership. Since the musta'min's aman ends upon entering enemy territory, the judge can no longer compel him to sell the slave; thus the alternative of forced emancipation applies. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, holding that the musta'min's ownership continues because the normal means of removal (sale) is unavailable, not that the slave becomes free. The text answers an objection that ihraz (seizure) normally confirms ownership, so it should not destroy it; the rebuttal distinguishes between ownership by capture (which begins with seizure) and ownership by purchase (which pre-exists). The second case: a slave of a harbi (enemy combatant) embraces Islam while still in dar al-harb and then escapes to Muslim territory. He becomes free based on the hadith of Ibn Abbas about the Prophet freeing the slaves of Thaqif who came to him, and because he has secured his own freedom by fleeing. This is conditional on him actually reaching Muslim territory. The third case: if a slave of a harbi becomes Muslim in dar al-harb and then Muslims conquer that territory, he also becomes free, because his own hand (control over himself) is prior in establishment, and his need for agency outweighs the Muslims' need to establish control over him. In all three cases, the slave is free without any formal manumission, and no wala' (clientage) is established, nor any hadd punishment for any infraction.

Key Terms
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim who is in a state of war with the Muslims, i.e., not under a treaty or aman.
عتقاء الله ('utaqa' Allah): Freed ones of God – referring to slaves who become free by divine decree through embracing Islam and escaping to Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the essential condition for a slave of a harbi to become free after embracing Islam?
A) He must declare his faith publicly in the marketplace.
B) He must physically leave dar al-harb and come to the Muslims.
C) He must kill his master.
D) He must wait for the Muslim army to conquer his area.

Key Takeaway
Embracing Islam alone does not free a slave of a harbi; he must also physically escape to Muslim territory to gain freedom.

Slave of Harbi Embracing Islam and Muslim Conquest
Ruling with proof: case based on hadith and qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a slave of a harbi becomes free if Muslims conquer his territory after his conversion, and evaluate the reasoning that his own hand takes precedence over the Muslims' hand.

Core Idea
A slave who embraces Islam in dar al-harb becomes free when Muslims conquer that territory, because his own claim to freedom precedes the Muslims' right to capture.

This lesson covers the third case from the passage: if a slave of a harbi embraces Islam in dar al-harb and later the Muslims conquer that territory, the slave becomes free. The text offers an interesting legal reasoning: the slave's own 'hand' (control over himself) is prior in establishment to the Muslims' hand, so his freedom is recognized first. This lesson explores that logic and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A slave of a harbi converts to Islam while still in dar al-harb, but does not escape. Later, the Muslim army conquers that territory.
2
State the ruling
The slave becomes free upon conquest. He does not become part of the war booty.
3
Understand the proof
The same hadith of Ibn Abbas is cited, and additionally a rational argument: the slave's own hand (control) is prior to the Muslims' hand.
4
Analyze the priority of hands
The slave's hand over himself was established at his birth (as a free person) or by his conversion; the Muslims' hand over them as conquerors would be established only at conquest. So the earlier hand takes precedence.
The phrase 'اعتبار يده أولى' is key.

Why does the slave become free upon conquest rather than being considered part of the booty?

Jihad — Armed Conflict Rules
intermediate
8 min
Ruling on a Harbi Slave Who Becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb
Ruling on three scenarios for a harbi slave who converts to Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios for a harbi slave who converts to Islam and identify the conditions that trigger automatic freedom.

Core Idea
A harbi slave who converts to Islam is freed only if he emigrates or Muslims conquer his land.

This lesson examines the legal status of a slave belonging to a non-Muslim enemy (harbi) who embraces Islam while still in enemy territory. The Hanafi school, drawing on a prophetic precedent and a rational principle, distinguishes three possible outcomes. Understanding this ruling clarifies how Islamic law handles conversion and liberation in the context of armed conflict.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the passage and list the three situations described: (1) the slave converts and emigrates to dar al-islam or the Muslim army; (2) the slave converts but does not emigrate; (3) the slave converts and then the Muslims conquer the land.
The text uses 'fajā'anā' (so he came to us) and 'aẓharnā ʿalayhim' (we conquered them) to mark the scenarios.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For scenario 1, the slave is free. For scenario 2, he remains a slave. For scenario 3, he is free. Note that in all three the slave has converted, but the outcome depends on emigration or conquest.
3
Analyze the primary proof (dalil naqli)
The passage cites the hadith of Ibn Abbas: the Prophet freed the slaves of Ta'if who came to him after accepting Islam. This establishes that conversion plus emigration produces freedom. Identify how the Hanafi jurists use this as evidence for scenario 1.
The hadith is narrated by Ahmad; it is ahad but accepted in this ruling.
4
Analyze the rational proof (dalil aqli)
For scenario 3 (conquest), the text adds a reasoning: the slave's own hand (i.e., his right over himself) preceded the Muslims' claim to him as booty. His conversion tips the balance in favor of his freedom. Identify this "precedence of the hand" argument.
The phrase 'wa iʿtibāru yadihi awlā min iʿtibāri yadi l-muslimīn' is key.
5
Note the consequences: no formal manumission, no wala', no hadd
In all cases where freedom is granted, it is automatic—no need for a manumission act. Additionally, no wala' (clientage) attaches to the freed person, and no hadd punishment is applied (presumably for any prior offense).
This highlights the unique nature of this freedom: it is a gift from Allah, not from a human master.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استيلاء الكفار وأحكامه: أو آمن عبد ثم، فجاءنا، أو ظهرنا عليهم عتق. أي أسلم عبد حربي. أو آمن عبد هذه مسألة ثانية أي إذا أسلم عبد الحربي في دار الحرب ثم خرج إلى دار الإسلام أو إلى عسكر المسلمين، فهو حر؛ لما روي عن ابن عباس لما قال: "أعتق رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم الطائف من خرج إليه من عبيد المشركين"، رواه أحمد أي من أسلم وخرج إليه فقضى بعتقه، وقال: "هم عتقاء الله تعالى"؛ ولأنه أحرز نفسه بالخروج إلينا مراغماً لمولاه، وقيد بقوله: "فجاءنا"؛ لأنه إن لم يخرج إلينا بعد الإسلام فهو عبد على حاله ولا يعتق. فجاءنا: أي العبد وخرج إلى دار الإسلام أو إلى عسكر المسلمين. أو ظهرنا عليهم: هذه مسألة ثالثة أي إذا أسلم عبد الحربي في دار الحرب، وغلبنا على أهل الحرب الذي هو عندهم عتق أيضاً؛ لما روينا، ولأنه التحق بمنعة المسلمين واعتبار يده أولى من اعتبار يد المسلمين؛ لأنها أسبق ثبوتاً على نفسه، والحاجة في حقه إلى زيادة التوكيل وفي حقهم إلى إثبات اليد ابتداء فلهذا كان اعتبار يده أولى. عتق أي العبد في الصور كلها بلا إعتاق ولا ولاء لا حد عليه.
Subjugation of the disbelievers and its rulings: Or if a slave [of a harbi] accepted Islam, then he came to us, or we conquered them, he becomes free. That is, a harbi slave accepted Islam. 'Or if a slave accepted Islam' is a second issue: if a harbi slave accepts Islam in dar al-harb, then goes out to dar al-islam or to the Muslim army, he is free; because of what was narrated from Ibn Abbas when he said: 'The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, freed the slaves of Ta'if who came out to him from among the slaves of the polytheists.' Ahmad narrated it, meaning whoever accepted Islam and came out to him, he ruled his freedom, and said: 'They are the freed ones of Allah the Exalted.' And because he secured himself by coming out to us in defiance of his master. And it is qualified by his saying 'so he came to us' because if he does not come out to us after accepting Islam, he remains a slave in his state and is not freed. 'So he came to us' means the slave came out to dar al-islam or to the Muslim army. 'Or we conquered them' is a third issue: if a harbi slave accepts Islam in dar al-harb, and we overcome the people of war who are there, he is also free; because of what we narrated, and because he has joined the protection of the Muslims, and consideration of his hand takes precedence over consideration of the Muslims' hand because it is earlier in establishment over his own self, and the need on his part is for additional empowerment, while on their part it is for initial establishment of control, therefore his hand is given precedence. 'Freed' means the slave is freed in all the forms, without manumission, without clientage, and there is no [hadd] punishment on him.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on the subjugation of disbelievers. It addresses the status of a slave belonging to a harbi (non-Muslim enemy) who embraces Islam while still in dar al-harb. The Hanafi school distinguishes three scenarios: (1) The slave accepts Islam and then escapes to dar al-islam or to the Muslim army. In this case, he is automatically freed based on the hadith of Ibn Abbas that the Prophet freed the slaves of Ta'if who came to him after accepting Islam. The underlying reason (sabab) is that the slave has secured his own freedom by emigrating in defiance of his master. (2) The slave accepts Islam but does not emigrate; he remains enslaved. (3) The Muslims conquer the land where the slave is, after his conversion. In this case too he becomes free because he has effectively joined the Muslim community and his own right over himself (his hand) is stronger than the Muslims' right to take him as booty, since his ownership over himself preceded their possession. The text emphasizes that in all these cases, the slave is freed without any formal manumission, without wala' (clientage) to anyone, and no hadd punishment applies (presumably for any prior offense). This is a settled Hanafi position.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Freedom; the slave becomes free automatically without formal manumission.
مراغماً (muraghiman): In defiance of his master; the slave flees to dar al-islam with the intention of escaping his master's authority.
ولاء (wala'): Clientage; the bond of loyalty between a freed slave and the manumittor. Here, no wala' is established.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what happens to a harbi slave who converts to Islam in dar al-harb and then emigrates to dar al-islam?
A) He remains a slave of his original master.
B) He becomes free automatically without manumission.
C) He becomes free only if his master also converts.
D) He is killed as a deserter.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a harbi slave who converts to Islam is freed automatically upon emigration to dar al-islam or upon Muslim conquest, based on the prophetic precedent and the principle that his own right over himself precedes the Muslims' right of capture.

Ruling on a Muslim Merchant Under Aman in Dar al-Harb: Prohibition of Treachery
Defines musta'man and spells out rulings on a Muslim merchant's conduct under aman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a Muslim merchant under aman is prohibited from harming harbis (haram), identify the exception when the harbis breach the pact, and explain the ownership and disposal of property taken by treachery.

Core Idea
Under aman, harming harbis is haram (treachery); but if they breach the pact, retaliation is permitted and property taken becomes charity.

This passage from the chapter on the musta'man (one who seeks aman) addresses a critical question in the law of siyar: what are the limits of a Muslim merchant's conduct when he enters enemy territory with a guarantee of safety (aman)? The text presents the default prohibition, its basis in the prohibition of treachery, and the nuanced outcome if property is nevertheless taken—ownership is valid but the property is considered 'impure' and must be given in charity. Understanding this ruling requires following the Hanafi reasoning from the hadith to the analogue of a fasid sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its prohibition
Locate the statement that a Muslim merchant under aman is forbidden from harming any property or person of the harbis. This is the hukm: haram. Note the condition 'as long as they adhere to their conditions.'
The prohibition is absolute until the harbis themselves commit treachery.
2
Examine the dalil (proof) for the prohibition
The text quotes the Prophet's hadith about the banner of the treacherous. Understand why this hadith indicates that harming under aman is treachery (ghadr), which is a major sin.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), but sufficient for prohibition.
3
Identify the exception to the prohibition
The text states an exception: if the harbi ruler or his deputy breaches the pact (e.g., by taking property or jizya), the merchant may retaliate against their property and lives, but not engage in sexual relations with captives.
The exception is based on the harbis' own treachery; the merchant's aman is then void.
4
Analyze the case of property brought to dar al-islam
Even if the merchant takes property by treachery and brings it to dar al-islam, he owns it because istila' (taking possession) creates ownership. However, the property is 'impure' due to the prohibited means, so he must give it in charity.
This is analogous to a fasid sale: ownership is established but the cause of acquisition is defective.
5
Understand the rule for debts between merchant and harbi
If there are credit transactions or torts between them, and both come to dar al-islam, the qadi does not enforce any claim. This is because the harbi is not subject to dar al-islam's jurisdiction, and the rights are not actionable.
This part is incomplete in the text; it indicates the principle that dar al-islam does not adjudicate claims against harbis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المستأمن أي في بيان أحكامه. دخل تاجرنا دار الحرب، حرم تعرّضه لشيء منهم، فلو أخرج شيئاً ملكه محظوراً، فيتصدق به، فإن ادانه حربي. أي دار الحرب أي تعرض التاجر أهل دار الحرب. ملكاً محظوراً خبيثاً. الاستئمان: طلب الأمان من العدو، وبعد حصول الأمان مستأمن بفتح الميم سواء كان حربياً أو معصوماً، وطلب الأمان على ضربين: دخولنا إليهم بالأمان، ودخولهم إلينا به فقدم استيمنان المسلم على الكافر. تاجرنا: أي تاجر المسلمين بأمان. حرم تعرضه لشيء: أي إذا دخل المسلم دار الحرب تاجراً، فلا يحل له أن يتعرض لشيء من أموالهم ولا من دمائهم؛ لأنه ضمن بالاستيمان أن لا يتعرض لهم، والتعرض بعد ذلك يكون غدراً، والغدر حرام، قال عليه السلام: 'لكل غادر لواء يغرز عند باب إسته يوم القيامة يعرف به غدره'، إلا إذا غدر ملكهم بأخذ الأموال أو الجنس أو غيره، أو فعل ذلك غير الملك بعلم الملك، ولم يمنعه، فحينئذ يباح له التعرض. فيجوز له أخذ أموالهم وقتل نفوسهم؛ لأنهم هم الذين نقضوا العهد، وليس له أن يستبيح فروجهم، فإن الفروج لا تحل إلا بالملك، ولا ملك قبل الإحراز بالدار، إلا إذا وجد امرأته المأسورة أو أم ولده أو مدبرته ولم يطأهن أهل الحرب؛ لأنهم لا يملكونها بالاستيلاء، غير أنهم إذا وطؤوها، يكون شبهة في حقهن، فيجب عليهن العدة، فلا يجوز له أن يطأهن حتى تنقضي عدتهن بخلاف أمته المأسورة حيث لا يجوز له أن يطأها وإن لم يطأها الحربي؛ لأنهم ملكوها، فصارت من جملة أموالهم. منهم ما داموا على شروطهم. فلو أخرج شيئاً أي لو أخرج التاجر شيئاً من أموالهم وأنفسهم إلينا ملكه ملكاً خبيثاً، فيتصدق به، أما ملكه؛ فلورود الاستيلاء على مال مباح، وأما كونه محظوراً؛ فلأنه حصل بسبب الغدر، فأوجب ذلك خبثاً فيه، فيؤمر بالتصدق به؛ وهذا لأن الحظر لغيره لا يمنع انعقاد سبب الملك كالبيع الفاسد، وقيد بالإخراج؛ لأنه إذا غصب شيئاً في دار الحرب ولم يخرجه، وجب عليه التوبة، وهي لا تحصل إلا بالرد عليهم، فأشبه المشتري شراء فاسداً. فيتصدق به أي بذلك الشيء الذي أخرجه وجوبا. فإن ادانه حربي [أي باع حربي بالدين من التاجر] يعني باشر حربي تصرفات أوجب الدين في ذمة التاجر، وفي الكفاية: الإدانة: البيع بالدين، والاستدانة: الابتياع بالدين وادان بتشديد الدال من باب الافتعال أي قبل الدين والدين غير القرض؛ إذ القرض لما يقرض فيقبض، والدين اسم لما دينا يصير في الذمة بالعقد أو الاستهلاك أي إذا دخل المسلم التاجر دار الحرب بأمان، فباع منه حربي شيئاً بالدين، أو باع التاجر حربياً شيئاً بالدين، أو غصب أحدهما شيئاً من الآخر، ثم خرجا إلينا من دار الحرب، وتحاكما إلى القاضي، فلا يقضي الحاكم بشيء لأحدهما على الآخر.
Chapter of the Musta'man: that is, explaining its rulings. Our merchant entered dar al-harb, his harming any of them is forbidden. So if he takes out something, he owns it as prohibited, and he gives it in charity. If a harbi gives him credit. That is, dar al-harb, that is, the merchant's harming the people of dar al-harb. [He owns it as] a prohibited, impure ownership. Istiman: seeking aman from the enemy, and after obtaining aman, he is musta'man (with fath of the mim) whether he is a harbi or a protected person. Seeking aman is of two types: our entering into their territory with aman, and their entering into ours with it, and the aman of the Muslim over the disbeliever is given precedence. 'Our merchant' means a Muslim merchant with aman. 'His harming any of them is forbidden': if a Muslim enters dar al-harb as a merchant, it is not permissible for him to harm any of their property or their lives, because he guaranteed by the aman that he would not harm them, and harming after that is treachery, and treachery is forbidden. The Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'For every treacherous person there is a banner that will be planted at the opening of his backside on the Day of Resurrection, by which his treachery will be recognized.' Unless their king commits treachery by taking property, or the jizya, or something else, or someone other than the king does that with the king's knowledge and he does not prevent him, then at that time it becomes permissible for him to harm them. So it is permissible for him to take their property and kill their persons, because they are the ones who broke the covenant. But he is not allowed to take their private parts (i.e., have sexual relations), because private parts are only permitted through ownership, and there is no ownership before actual control in the land, unless he finds his captured wife, or his umm walad, or his mudabbarah, and the people of war have not had intercourse with them, because they do not own them by mere control. However, if they did have intercourse with them, there is a doubt (shubha) regarding their [the women's] rights, so they must observe an 'iddah, and it is not permissible for him to have intercourse with them until their 'iddah expires. This is different from his captured slave girl, where it is not permissible for him to have intercourse with her even if the harbi did not have intercourse with her, because they owned her, so she became part of their property. [He must not harm them] as long as they adhere to their conditions. So if he takes out something, meaning if the merchant takes out something from their property or their persons to us, he owns it as impure ownership, and he gives it in charity. As for his ownership, it is because of the occurrence of control over permissible property; and as for it being prohibited, it is because it was obtained through treachery, which necessitated impurity in it. So he is ordered to give it in charity. This is because an external prohibition does not prevent the cause of ownership from being established, like a fasid sale. And it is restricted to taking out, because if he usurps something in dar al-harb and does not take it out, he must repent, and repentance is only achieved by returning it to them, similar to a buyer in a fasid purchase. So he gives it in charity, i.e., that thing which he took out, as an obligation. If a harbi gives him credit, meaning a harbi sold on credit to the merchant, or the merchant sold on credit to the harbi, or one of them usurped something from the other, then both come out to us from dar al-harb and go to court, the judge does not decide anything in favor of one against the other.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the musta'man (one who seeks aman). It first defines istiman as seeking security from the enemy, and once granted, the person is called musta'man. There are two types: Muslims entering dar al-harb with aman, and harbis entering dar al-islam with aman. The chapter focuses on the first type: a Muslim merchant who enters dar al-harb with aman. The core ruling is that it is haram for him to harm any person or property of the harbis as long as they adhere to the conditions of the aman, because doing so would be treachery (ghadr), which is a grave sin. The Prophet's hadith about the banner of treachery underscores this. However, if the harbi ruler or someone else under his authority breaches the aman (e.g., by taking the merchant's property or jizya), the merchant is then permitted to retaliate by taking their property and lives, but not by sexual relations with women, as ownership of captives requires actual control (ihraz) in dar al-islam. There are special rules for finding his own wife or slave-concubine. The passage then addresses a situation where the merchant takes property from the harbis and brings it to dar al-islam. He becomes the owner of that property, but because it was obtained through treachery, it is considered impure (khabith) and he must give it in charity. This is analogous to a fasid (voidable) sale where ownership is established despite the prohibition due to an external factor. Finally, if there are debts between the merchant and a harbi (e.g., a sale on credit or a tort), and both come to dar al-islam and take the case to a qadi, the judge does not enforce any claim for either party. This is because the rights are not subject to enforcement in dar al-islam due to the lack of jurisdiction over the harbi or the uncertain nature of the transaction. The passage ends mid-explanation but the ruling is clear.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'man): One who seeks aman (security) from the enemy; after receiving aman, he is a musta'man, whether he is originally a harbi or a protected person.
غدر (ghadr): Treachery; breaking a promise of security. It is haram and a major sin.
إدانة (idanah): Credit transaction; selling on credit or buying on credit. Here, it refers to any transaction that creates a debt in the dhimma.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a Muslim merchant under aman in dar al-harb takes some property belonging to a harbi and brings it to dar al-islam, what must he do according to the Hanafi ruling in this passage?
A) Return it to the harbi.
B) Keep it as permissible spoils.
C) Give it in charity.
D) Destroy it.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim merchant under aman must not harm harbis; if he does, he owns what he takes but must give it in charity because of the sin of treachery.

Diyah and Kaffarah for Muslim Musta'min in Dar al-Harb
Ruling on diya and kaffara for Muslim musta'min homicide in Dar al-Harb with intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diya and kaffara for Muslim musta'min in Dar al-Harb and explain why qisas is dropped, and evaluate the opposing arguments from Abu Yusuf and the three schools.

Core Idea
In Dar al-Harb, qisas drops because enforcement is impossible; diyah remains because inviolability persists.

This lesson examines a nuanced case where two Muslims enter enemy territory with aman and one kills the other. The Hanafi school distinguishes between diyah and qisas based on the practical impossibility of enforcing qisas without Muslim authority. We will explore the reasoning behind dropping qisas and the obligation of diyah and kaffarah, as well as the differing views of Abu Yusuf and the three other imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
Understand that both individuals are Muslim musta'min (given safe passage) in Dar al-Harb. One kills the other, either intentionally or by mistake.
The status of musta'min preserves inviolability.
2
State the Hanafi ruling and its components
According to the matn, diyah is wajib from the killer's wealth in both intention and error, and kaffarah is wajib only in error. Qisas is dropped.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
3
Understand the reasoning for dropping qisas
The Hanafi argument: qisas requires the force (mana'ah) of the imam and community, which is absent in Dar al-Harb. Thus execution is impossible, so obligation is futile. Also, Dar al-Harb is a domain where blood is permitted, creating a shubha (doubt) that prevents qisas.
Inability to execute (ta'addhur al-istifa') is the core reason.
4
Compare with opposing views
Abu Yusuf holds qisas applies because the heir can execute it individually. The three schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) also require qisas for intentional killing, citing that inviolability is not lost by location.
The dispute hinges on whether individual enforcement suffices.
5
Derive the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi law substitutes diyah for qisas; non-Hanafi schools execute the killer if caught, even in Dar al-Harb.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the position of the matn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسلمان مستأمنان قتل أحدهما صاحبه تجبُ الدية في ماله، والكفارة في الخطأ، سواء كان عمدا أو خطأ. وتجب الكفارة أيضاً دون العمد - فلأن القضاء يعتمد الولاية... وأما الدية؛ فلأنه لم تجب القصاص وقت القتل، لتعذر الاستيفاء؛ لأنه إنما يكون بالمنعة، ولا منعة دون الإمام وجماعة المسلمين، ولم يوجد ذلك في دار الحرب، فتجب الدية؛ لأن العصمة لا تبطل بالدخول في دار الحرب؛ لأنها ثابتة بالإسلام، فلا تبطل بعارض الدخول بالأمان لا بالكفر، وأما وجوبها في ماله في صورة العمد؛ لأن العاقلة لا تعقل العمد؛ لما عرف في موضعه، وأما في صورة الخطأ؛ فلأنه لا قدرة بهم على الصيانة مع تباين الدارين، والوجوب عليهم إنما هو على اعتبار ترك الصيانة الواجبة عليهم. وأما الكفارة أيضاً في الخطأ فلإطلاق قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ قَتَلَ مُؤْمِناً خَطَأَ فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٍ وَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ﴾ (النساء:٩٢) لم يقيد بدار الحرب أو بدار الإسلام، وعند الثلاثة تجب الدية في الخطأ والقصاص في العمد، وعن أبي يوسف يجب القصاص عليه؛ لأن بدخول دار الحرب لا تبطل العصمة، والقصاص حق الولي ينفرد باستيفائه. ولنا: أن الواحد لا يقاوم القاتل ظاهراً، فلا فائدة في الوجوب بدون إمكان الاستيفاء، فسقط القصاص، ولأن دار الحرب دار إباحة الدم فيصير ذلك شبهة.
Two Muslim musta'min, one kills the other: the diyah is obligatory from his wealth, and the kaffarah in case of error, whether it was intentional or by mistake. And kaffarah is also [required] in error, not in intentional. As for diyah, because qisas was not obligatory at the time of killing due to inability to execute it; it can only be with force, and there is no force without the imam and the Muslim community, and that was not present in Dar al-Harb. So diyah is obligatory because inviolability is not nullified by entering Dar al-Harb; it is established by Islam, so it is not nullified by the incidental entry with aman, not by kufr. And its obligation from his wealth in the case of intent is because the 'aqila do not bear responsibility for intentional killing, as known in its place. And in the case of error, because they have no ability to protect [the victim] due to the separation of realms, and the obligation on them [the 'aqila] is based on neglecting the duty of protection. As for kaffarah in error, it is due to the general application of Allah's saying: 'And whoever kills a believer by mistake, then the freeing of a believing slave and a diyah to his family' (Surah an-Nisa 4:92) — it was not restricted to Dar al-Harb or Dar al-Islam. According to the three [imams], diyah is obligatory in error and qisas in intent. And from Abu Yusuf, qisas is obligatory because entering Dar al-Harb does not nullify inviolability, and qisas is the right of the heir, who can execute it individually. Our [Hanafi] evidence: a single person cannot overpower the killer apparently, so there is no benefit in obligation without possibility of execution, thus qisas drops, and because Dar al-Harb is a domain where blood is permitted, so that becomes a doubt [shubha].
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage deals with the case where two Muslim musta'min (who entered Dar al-Harb with aman) are present, and one kills the other, either intentionally or by mistake. The Hanafi ruling, which is the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, states that diyah (blood money) is obligatory from the killer's own wealth in both cases, and kaffarah (expiation) is only required in accidental killing. The reason is that qisas (retaliation) cannot be executed due to lack of capability: in Dar al-Harb, there is no Muslim imam or community to enforce it, and the victim's heir cannot do it alone effectively. Therefore, diyah replaces qisas in intentional killing as well. The kaffarah for error is based on the general Quranic verse (4:92), which applies regardless of location. The passage then presents the view of the 'three' (likely Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali) that in Dar al-Harb, qisas is obligatory in intentional killing because inviolability remains intact. Abu Yusuf's opinion within the Hanafi school also supports qisas, reasoning that the heir can execute qisas independently. The Hanafi response is that without the imam's support, execution is practically impossible, and the permissibility of blood in Dar al-Harb creates a legal doubt (shubha) that prevents qisas. Thus, only diyah remains.

Key Terms
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money payable to the victim's heirs for homicide, replacing qisas when qisas is not possible.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation for accidental killing, consisting of freeing a believing slave or fasting two consecutive months.
المنعة (al-mana'ah): The power and authority of the Muslim community/imam to enforce penal laws, including qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school drop qisas for a Muslim musta'min who intentionally kills another musta'min in Dar al-Harb?
A) Because the victim's inviolability is lost in Dar al-Harb.
B) Because the killer cannot be identified.
C) Because there is no imam or Muslim community to enforce qisas, making execution impossible.
D) Because the killer must pay diyah first.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, qisas is not enforced in Dar al-Harb due to inability to execute, so diyah replaces it in both intentional and accidental killing.

Muslim Prisoners Killing Each Other in Dar al-Harb: Qisas and Diyah
Intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute on qisas and diyah for prisoners in dar al-harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on qisas and diyah when Muslim prisoners kill each other in dar al-harb, and explain the dispute with the Sahibayn and other schools.

Core Idea
In dar al-harb, a Muslim prisoner loses complete immunity (ismah mutaqawwimah) but retains punitive immunity (ismah mu'ththimah).

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute: if two Muslim prisoners in enemy territory kill each other, what are the legal consequences? The matn states a stark ruling – only kaffarah in error, nothing else – but the commentary reveals significant disagreement among the Hanafi masters themselves. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the Hanafi distinction between two types of immunity and the practical limits of Islamic penal law in dar al-harb.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifah)
Identify the ruling as given in the matn: no diyah, no qisas, only kaffarah for mistaken killing. Note that for intentional killing, even kaffarah is not due according to the Hanafi school (as mentioned in the commentary).
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi difference (Sahibayn)
State that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) hold that diyah is due from the killer's own wealth in both intentional and mistaken killing, though they agree that qisas is not obligatory.
Abu Yusuf also individually held qisas is due, but this is not adopted.
3
Follow the Sahibayn's proof
Understand their reasoning: captivity does not nullify immunity (ismah), and diyah is the right of the heirs; qisas is not possible due to lack of protection, but diyah remains as compensation.
They analogize to the musta'min (one with aman).
4
Analyze Abu Hanifah's response
Examine Abu Hanifah's dalil: the prisoner becomes a dependent of the enemy, so complete immunity (ismah mutaqawwimah) is lost; dar al-harb is not a place for exacting Islamic penal laws, creating a shubhah that prevents qisas and diyah. Only punitive immunity (ismah mu'ththimah) remains, obligating kaffarah for error.
The Quranic verse (4:92) is cited for kaffarah, but diyah is not extended due to lack of ihraz (protective control).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifah, the killer pays nothing financially (only kaffarah in error); under Sahibayn, he pays diyah from his own wealth. The matn and commentary adopt Abu Hanifah's view as mufta bihi.
Also compare with the three imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) who require qisas/diyah as in dar al-Islam.

غيرهم. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) وقتل مسلم مسلماً أسلم ثمه: [ أي كما لا شيء الكفارة في الخطأ في قتل مسلم. (عيني)] هذه المسألة اتفاقية بين أصحابنا أي إذا قتل المسلم مسلماً أسلم في دار الحرب ولم يهاجر إلينا، فعليه الكفارة في الخطأ لا غير؛ لعدم العصمة المتقومة، وبقاء العصمة المؤثمة، والدليل عليه قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنْ كَانَ مِنْ قَوْمٍ عَدُوٍّ لَكُمْ وَهُوَ مُؤْمِنٌ فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٍ (النساء: ۹۲) الآية، ولعدم الاحراز بدارنا، وعند الشافعي: يجب القصاص بقتله عمداً، والدية بقتله خطأ؛ لأنه قتل نفساً معصومة، وبه قال مالك وأحمد ثم اعلم أن دار الحرب تمنع وجوب ما يندراً بالشبهات؛ لأن أحكامنا لا تجري في دارهم وحكم دارهم لا يجري في دارنا حتى لو أسلم حربي في دار الحرب وقتل مسلماً دخل دارهم بأمان، لا يجب القصاص، خلافاً للشافعي، وكذا المسلم إذا شرب الخمر أو زنى أو قذف في دارهم لا يجب الحد عندنا، خلافاً له. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني)
There is nothing (due) regarding the two prisoners except the expiation in case of error, and (the case of) a Muslim killing a Muslim who had embraced Islam then (emigrated?)... from the blood money and retaliation. And the obligation upon them is only based on neglecting the required protection. As for the expiation also in case of error, it is due to the generality of His statement: 'And whoever kills a believer by mistake, then freeing a believing slave and compensation (diyah) delivered to his family' (An-Nisa 4:92), which is not restricted to dar al-harb or dar al-Islam. According to the three (imams), diyah is due in error and qisas in intentional killing. And from Abu Yusuf, qisas is obligatory upon him, because entering dar al-harb does not nullify immunity (ismah), and qisas is the right of the heir who alone may exact it. Our proof: that one person cannot resist the killer openly, so there is no benefit in obligation without possibility of exacting it, so qisas falls; and because dar al-harb is a place where blood is permitted, so that creates a doubt (shubhah). (Mukhtasar, Fath 'Ayni) Regarding the two Muslim prisoners: if one kills the other, whether intentionally or by mistake... (T) 'except the expiation in error': i.e., even if two Muslims are prisoners in dar al-harb and one kills the other, then nothing is due from the 'aqilah of diyah, nor from the killer of qisas according to Abu Hanifah. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: the killer must pay diyah from his own wealth in both error and intent, as in the case of the musta'min, because they are truly people of our dar, and immunity is not nullified by the incidental captivity just as it is not nullified by the incidental aman, except that qisas is not obligatory due to lack of protection, and dar al-harb is not a place for exacting punitive penalties; and diyah is due from his wealth due to absence of 'aqilah and because they do not bear responsibility for intentional killing. Abu Hanifah's proof: that through captivity he becomes a dependent of them, as evidenced by the fact that he becomes resident by their residence and traveler by their travel, and dar al-harb is not a place for exacting Islamic rulings, so the protective control (ihraz) is invalidated, and thus the complete immunity (al-ismah al-mutaqawwimah) falls, which is that which necessitates financial compensation when violated; therefore diyah is not due in either intentional or error. But the punitive immunity (al-ismah al-mu'ththimah) remains, which is that which necessitates sin when violated, so expiation is due; in error, expiation (kaffarah) is due because there is no expiation in intentional killing according to us, as passed in the chapter of oaths. And according to their view (the two), the three (imams) held. Then the author analogized this disputed issue among our scholars to an agreed issue among them, so the difference is in the following issue between them and others. (Mukhtasar, Fath, 'Ayni) 'And killing a Muslim who embraced Islam then (emigrated?)': [i.e., just as nothing (is due) except expiation in error in killing a Muslim. ('Ayni)] This issue is agreed upon among our scholars: i.e., if a Muslim kills a Muslim who embraced Islam in dar al-harb and did not emigrate to us, then upon him is expiation in error only, due to lack of complete immunity and remaining punitive immunity; the evidence is His statement: 'But if he was from a people who are enemies to you and he is a believer, then freeing a believing slave' (An-Nisa 4:92) and because of lack of protective control in our dar. According to al-Shafi'i: qisas is obligatory for intentional killing and diyah for mistaken killing because he killed a protected soul, and Malik and Ahmad also said this. Then know that dar al-harb prevents the obligation of what is rare due to doubts, because our rulings do not apply in their land and their rulings do not apply in our land; so even if a harbi embraces Islam in dar al-harb and kills a Muslim who entered their land with aman, no qisas is obligatory, contrary to al-Shafi'i. Likewise, if a Muslim drinks wine, commits zina, or slanders in their land, the hadd penalty is not obligatory according to us, contrary to him. (Mukhtasar, Fath, 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when two Muslim prisoners in dar al-harb kill each other. The matn states: 'Nothing is due from the two prisoners except the expiation in case of error.' The commentary expands this ruling, presenting the intra-Hanafi dispute and the disagreement with other schools.

The ruling according to Abu Hanifah: Neither diyah nor qisas is owed; only kaffarah for mistaken killing. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that diyah is due from the killer's own wealth in both intentional and mistaken killing, but no qisas. The three imams (al-Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) hold that diyah is due for error and qisas for intent.

The key Hanafi proof: Dar al-harb is not a place for exacting Islamic penal rulings, and the captive Muslims lack full immunity (ismah mutaqawwimah) because they are under enemy control. However, they retain punitive immunity (ismah mu'ththimah), so sin and kaffarah apply. Abu Yusuf's individual opinion that qisas is due is mentioned but not adopted.

Then the passage draws an analogy to the agreed-upon case of killing a Muslim who embraced Islam in dar al-harb but did not emigrate: here only kaffarah is due, and the Quranic verse (4:92) is cited. The Shafi'i position requiring qisas or diyah is rejected because the deceased lacked full immunity due to not being under Islamic jurisdiction.

Key Terms
عصمة متقومة (al-ismah al-mutaqawwimah): Complete immunity that entails financial compensation (diyah) if violated.
عصمة مؤثمة (al-ismah al-mu'ththimah): Punitive immunity that entails sin (ithm) and kaffarah if violated, but not financial compensation.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory under non-Muslim rule where Islamic rulings are not enforced.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifah, what is the ruling if a Muslim prisoner intentionally kills another Muslim prisoner in dar al-harb?
A) Qisas is obligatory.
B) Diyah is obligatory from the killer's wealth.
C) Only kaffarah is obligatory.
D) Nothing is obligatory – no qisas, no diyah, and no kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
In dar al-harb, a Muslim prisoner loses complete immunity (ismah mutaqawwimah) but retains punitive immunity (ismah mu'ththimah), so only kaffarah in error is due; for intentional killing, no worldly penalty applies.

Transition of Musta'min to Dhimmi After One Year
Ruling on musta'min becoming dhimmi after one year if warned

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a musta'min becomes a dhimmi and explain the Hanafi analogy to kharaj.

Core Idea
A musta'min becomes a dhimmi after one year if warned; staying silent implies acceptance of jizyah.

Not every extended stay by a musta'min results in dhimmi status. The Hanafi school establishes a clear trigger: a warning from the imam followed by a year's residence. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its underlying reasoning, including the qiyas to kharaj land tax, to help you understand how legal status changes in dar al-Islam.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the core legal consequence: the musta'min becomes a dhimmi and cannot return.
The ruling is conditional on a prior warning.
2
Identify the condition
What triggers the transition? The imam (or deputy) must explicitly tell the musta'min that staying a year will obligate jizyah.
Without warning, even years of stay do not make him a dhimmi.
3
Follow the qiyas argument
The passage analogizes jizyah to kharaj: accepting a financial obligation for land also indicates intent to reside permanently.
The illa (effective cause) is acceptance of a permanent financial duty.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What practical changes? Qisas, diyah, prohibition of harm, etc. apply between Muslim and dhimmi.
The musta'min is no longer a temporary visitor but a permanent resident.

فصل: في بيان ما بقي من أحكام المستأمن [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٢/١] لا يمكن مستأمن إلخ: [ أي الذي يدخل من أهل الحرب بأمان. (عيني)] والأصل فيه أن الكافر لا يمكن من إقامة دائمة في دارنا إلا باسترقاق أو جزية؛ لأنه يبقى ضرراً على المسلمين؛ لكونه جاسوساً لهم، وظهيراً على الأمور علينا ويمكن من الإقامة اليسيرة؛ لأن في منعها قطع المنافع من الطعام والجلب وسد باب التجارات ففصلنا بين المدتين بسنة؛ لأنها مدة تجب فيها الجزية، وقيد بالمستأمن؛ لأنه لو دخل دارنا بلا أمان فهو وما معه فيء لجماعة المسلمين. فينا: أي يقيم في دار الإسلام. وقيل له: أي من قبل الإمام أعم أن يكون القائل هو الإمام أو نائبه إن أقمت سنة: قيد اتفاقي؛ لجواز توقيت ما دونها كشهر وشهرين والجزية بكسر الجيم وسكون المعجمة: اسم للمال يؤخذ من الذمي، من الجزاء بمعنى القضاء؛ لأنه يجزى عن دمه عيني فتح وقيل له: أي للمستأمن من قبل الإمام. فإن مكث بعده: [ أي بعد ما قيل له ذلك. (ط)] هذا ظاهر في توقف كونه ذمياً على قول الإمام أو نائبه حتى أقام سنين من غير أن يقول له شيئاً كان له أن يرجع، ولا جزية في حول المكث إلا بالشرط، وقوله: فهو ذمي أي فيجري القصاص بينه وبين المسلم، ويضمن المسلم قيمة حمره وخنزيره بالإتلاف وتجب الدية عليه بقتله خطأ، ويجب كف الأذى عنه، وتحرم غيبته كالمسلم والذمي من الذمة بمعنى العهد ؛ لأنه عاهد المسلمين على ترك الحرب؛ ولأن نقضه يوجب الذم فالمستأمن لما أقام سنة بعد تقدم أمر الإمام إليه صار ملتزماً للجزية، فيصير ذمياً. ثم السنة أقصى هذه المدة وللإمام أن يوقت دون السنة، والأدنى غير مقدرة، بل مفوض إلى رأي الإمام، وقت التقدم إليه لا من وقت دخوله دار الإسلام فلم يترك أن لأن عقد الذمة خلف عن الإسلام فلا ينقض عهدها. (مستخلص فتح عيني إليهم : أي إلى دار الحرب؛ لأن في رجوعه إليهم ضرراً بالمسلمين لعوده حرباً عليهم. (عيني)

(عيني) كما لو وضع عليه الخراج أي إن دخل الحربي دارنا بأمان، واشتري أرض خراج، ووضع عليه خراج الأرض بأن ألزم به وأخذ عند حلول وقته بمباشرة سببه وهو زراعة الأرض أو التمكن هنا، يصير ذمياً؛ لأن خراج الأرض بمنزلة خراج الرأس، فإذا التزمه صار ملتزماً المقام في دارنا، فأما بمجرد الشراء فلا يصير ذميّا؛ لأن الشراء قد يكون للتجارة، وفيه إشارة إلى أنه لا يصير ذمياً بمجرد المدة وضع الخراج عليه، بل إنما يصير ذمياً بالتزام الخراج وبعد ذلك يلزمه الجزية للسنة المستقبلة، ويعتبر وقت وجوبه، وقيل: يصير ذمياً بنفس الشراء؛ لأنه حينئذ صار ملتزماً حكماً من أحكام الإسلام. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٣/١]
Chapter: On explaining what remains of the rulings of the musta'min. A musta'min cannot be allowed (to stay) among us for a year, and it is said to him: 'If you stay for a year, jizyah will be imposed upon you.' If he stays after that (warning) for a year, he is a dhimmi, and he is not left to return to them (dar al-harb), just as if kharaj is imposed upon him. (Fath 'Ayni) The chapter: explaining what remains of the rulings of the musta'min. 'A musta'min cannot be allowed...' i.e., he who enters from the people of war with aman. The principle is that a disbeliever cannot be allowed permanent residence in our dar except through enslavement or jizyah, because he remains a harm to the Muslims, as he may be a spy for them and a supporter against us. He may be allowed short-term residence because preventing it cuts off benefits like food, trade, etc. So we distinguished between the two durations by a year, because it is the duration in which jizyah becomes obligatory. It is restricted to the musta'min, because if he enters our dar without aman, he and his property become fay' for the Muslim community. 'Fina': i.e., he resides in dar al-Islam. 'And it is said to him': i.e., by the imam, whether the speaker is the imam or his deputy. 'If you stay for a year': this is a conventional limit; it is permissible to set a period less than that, like a month or two. 'Jizyah' is a noun for wealth taken from the dhimmi, derived from 'jaza'' meaning recompense, because it ransoms his blood. 'Fath' 'Then if he stays after that': [i.e., after that statement (T)] This indicates that his becoming a dhimmi depends on the statement of the imam or his deputy; if he stays years without being told anything, he may return, and no jizyah is due in the year of stay except by condition. 'So he is a dhimmi': i.e., qisas becomes applicable between him and a Muslim; a Muslim is liable for the value of his donkey and pig if destroyed; diyah is due if he kills him by mistake; harm must be prevented from him; backbiting him is prohibited as with a Muslim and a dhimmi. 'Dhimmi' from 'dhimmah' meaning covenant, because he covenants with the Muslims to abstain from war, and breaking it deserves blame. So when the musta'min stays a year after the imam's advance order, he becomes committed to jizyah and thus becomes a dhimmi. Then, the year is the maximum period; the imam may set a period less than a year; the minimum is not fixed but left to the imam's discretion. The period is counted from the time of the advance order, not from the time of his entry into dar al-Islam. So he is not allowed to return to them (dar al-harb) because the contract of dhimma is a substitute for Islam and cannot be violated. (Mukhtasar, Fath, 'Ayni) 'To them': i.e., to dar al-harb, because returning to them causes harm to Muslims by his becoming a harbi against them. ('Ayni) 'Just as if kharaj is imposed upon him': i.e., if a harbi enters our dar with aman and buys kharaj land, and kharaj is imposed on the land by obligating him and taking it at its due time upon the occurrence of its cause (cultivation or capability), he becomes a dhimmi, because land kharaj is like head kharaj (jizyah); so if he accepts it, he becomes committed to residence in our dar. But merely buying does not make him a dhimmi, because buying may be for trade. This indicates that he does not become a dhimmi merely by the period or imposition of kharaj; rather, he becomes a dhimmi by accepting kharaj, and after that, jizyah for the following year becomes obligatory; the time of its obligation is considered. It is said that he becomes a dhimmi by the purchase itself because then he has accepted a ruling of Islam.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage explains the transition of a musta'min (a non-Muslim from dar al-harb who enters dar al-Islam with temporary safe conduct) to a dhimmi (permanent protected resident). The ruling: If the imam tells the musta'min that he must pay jizyah if he stays a year, and the musta'min then stays a full year after that warning, he becomes a dhimmi and cannot return to dar al-harb. The year is the maximum; the imam can set a shorter period. The ruling is based on the principle that non-Muslims cannot reside permanently without paying jizyah (or being enslaved). The commentary analogizes this to the case where a musta'min buys kharaj land and is subject to kharaj: by accepting the kharaj obligation, he demonstrates intent to reside, thus becoming a dhimmi. However, mere buying without accepting kharaj does not suffice. The consequence of becoming a dhimmi is that qisas, diyah, and other Islamic rulings apply between him and Muslims.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim from dar al-harb who enters dar al-Islam with temporary safe conduct (aman).
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim permanent resident of dar al-Islam who pays jizyah and is protected by the Muslim state.
خراج (kharaj): Land tax imposed on agricultural land, often associated with non-Muslim ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required for a musta'min to become a dhimmi according to this passage?
A) He must stay in dar al-Islam for exactly one year from the day he entered.
B) He must be told by the imam that he will pay jizyah if he stays a year, and then he stays a year after that warning.
C) He must purchase land and pay kharaj.
D) He must explicitly declare that he wants to become a dhimmi.

Key Takeaway
A musta'min becomes a dhimmi only if the imam explicitly warns him about jizyah and he then stays a full year; without warning, he remains a musta'min.

Jizyah: Definition, Evidence, and Rates in the Hanafi School
Explanation of jizyah: definition, evidence, amounts, and refutation of an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of jizyah, identify its evidence from Quran and Sunnah, and calculate the amounts for poor, middle, and rich dhimmis according to the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
Jizyah is a compensation for protection, not a ransom for disbelief; its amount depends on the dhimmi's wealth category.

This lesson explains the Hanafi rulings on jizyah, the tax imposed on dhimmis as part of the Islamic state's protection covenant. We will examine the school's definition, its scriptural basis, and how the annual amounts are determined based on wealth categories. The passage also addresses an objection about jizyah's legitimacy and clarifies the wisdom behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that jizyah is a wajib annual tax on able-bodied male dhimmis as a condition of their protection (dhimmah).
The passage states jizyah is imposed on the 'poor able-bodied person' and others.
2
Examine the definition and etymology
Understand that the term 'jizyah' comes from 'tujzi'u' meaning suffices to prevent killing—it discharges the obligation to protect.
The text says: 'سميت بذلك؛ لأنها تجزئ عن الذمي أي تقضى وتكف عن القتل'.
3
Analyze the evidence (dalil)
Locate the Quranic verse (9:29) and the Sunnah (taking jizyah from Zoroastrians of Najran) as primary proofs.
Both are cited in the passage; the verse is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala regarding amount.
4
Understand the amounts based on wealth categories
Note the three categories: poor (12 dirhams/year), middle (24 dirhams/year), and rich (varies, but defined as owning 10,000 dirhams or more).
The passage details that for poor, it is 1 dirham/month; for middle, double that. The rich amount is not specified numerically but by the threshold.
5
Evaluate intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and the mufta bihi
Recognize the differences in defining the boundaries of the categories (e.g., using 200 dirhams, 10,000 dirhams, or custom). The relied-upon position clarifies that the wealthy threshold is 10,000 dirhams.
The text says: 'الغني من يملك عشرة آلاف درهم فصاعداً، وهو أحسن الأقوال، وعليه الاعتماد'.

Source Text

فصل أحكام الجزية أي في بيان أحكام الجزية الجزية لو وضعت بتراض، لا يعدل عنها، وإلا توضع على الفقير المعتمل في كلّ سنة اثنا عشر درهماً، وعلى وسط الحال ضعفه، في كل شهر درهم مي بعقد فصل: أي في الجزية وجمعه جزى، مثل اللحية واللحى، وسميت بذلك؛ لأنها تجزئ عن الذمي أي تقضى وتكف عن القتل؛ لأنه إذا قبلها سقط عنه القتل [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٧/١] وهي ثابتة بالكتاب، وهو قوله تعالى: حَتَّى يُعْطُوا الْجِزْيَةَ عَنْ يَدٍ وَهُمْ صَاغِرُونَ ﴾ (التوبة: ۲۹) وبالسنة وهو ما روي أنه عليه أخذ الجزية من محوس نجران. وقد طعن بعض الملحدين حيث قالوا: كيف يجوز تقرير الكفار على الكفر بمال، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز تقرير الزاني على الزنا بمال يؤخذ منه؟ وأجيب بأن الجزية لم تكن بدلا عن تقريره على الكفر، بل هي عوض عن ترك القتل والاسترقاق الواجبين، فجاز كإسقاط القصاص بعوض، وهي عقوبة على الكفر فيجوز كالاسترقاق أو الذمة ليسكن مع المسلمين فربما يرى محاسن الإسلام فيسلم مع أن فيها دفع شره. (فتح) بتراض بأن صالحهم الإمام على مبلغ معين من الدراهم والدنانير وغيرهما. (عيني) لا يعدل عنها: أي عن الجزية الموضوعة بالتراضي والصلح؛ لأنها تتقدر بحسب ما يقع عليه الاتفاق. رمز الحقائق: ٤٣٧/١] والموجب هو التراضي فلا يجوز التعدي منه إلى غيره ؛ ولأنه عليه صالح أهل نجران على ألفي حلة النصف في صفر، والنصف في رجب. ونجران اسم أرض في اليمن. وما في الهداية من أنه عليه صالح بني نجران على ألف من حي ومائتي حلة غير صحيح والصحيح على ألفي حلة. (فتح) وإلا توضع إلخ : أي وإن لم توضع الجزية بالتراضي بل بالقهر في ما إذا غلب الإمام على الكفار وأقرهم على أموالهم، فحينئذ توضع على الفقير المعتمل أي الصحيح القادر على الكسب في كل سنة اثنا عشر درهماً يؤخذ منه في كل شهر درهم حتى لو لم يعمل مع قدرته وجبت كمن عطل أرض الخراج، وقيل: الفقير المعتمل هو الذي يقدر على تحصيل الدراهم والدنانير بأي وجه كان وإن لم يحسن حرفة. (فتح) الفقير المعتمل: : هو الذي يكتسب أكثر من حاجة وقيل الذي يملك ما دون المائتين. (عيني) وعلى وسط الحال ضعفه [وهو الذي له مال ولكنه لا يستغني بماله عن الكسب. (عيني)] واعلم أن المعتبر في الغنا والفقر أكثر السنة، فلو كان غنياً في نصف الحول، وفقيراً في النصف يؤخذ منه جزية الوسط، والغني من يملك عشرة آلاف درهم فصاعداً، وهو أحسن الأقوال، وعليه الاعتماد، واعتبر أبو جعفر العرف، والمتوسط من يملك مائتي درهم إلى عشرة آلاف درهم وقيل: هو الذي له مال ولا يستغني عن الكسب والفقير من لا يملك مائتي درهم، وقيل: من لا بد له من الكسب لإصلاح معيشته والحاصل أنه إذا غلب الإمام على الكفار وأقرهم على أموالهم، =
Section: Rulings of Jizyah – i.e., explaining the rulings of jizyah. Jizyah: if it is imposed by mutual consent, one should not deviate from it; otherwise, it is imposed on the poor able-bodied person at twelve dirhams per year, and on the middle class double that, i.e., one dirham per month. (Explanation by word parsing) Section: i.e., on jizyah; its plural is juzā, like liḥyah and liḥā. It is named such because it suffices (tujzi'u) for the dhimmi, i.e., it discharges and prevents killing; because when he accepts it, killing is remitted from him. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/437] And it is established by the Book, namely the Almighty's saying: 'Until they give the jizyah willingly while they are humiliated' (Qur'an 9:29), and by the Sunnah: the narration that he (peace be upon him) took jizyah from the Zoroastrians of Najran. Some atheists have objected, saying: How is it permissible to confirm disbelievers in disbelief in exchange for money? If that were permissible, it would be permissible to confirm a fornicator in fornication for a sum taken from him. The answer: Jizyah is not a substitute for confirming him in disbelief; rather, it is compensation for leaving off killing and enslaving, which are obligatory, so it is permissible like dropping retaliation (qiṣāṣ) for compensation. It is a punishment for disbelief, so it is permissible like enslavement; or it is for protection (dhimmah) so that he may live among Muslims and perhaps see the beauties of Islam and become Muslim, besides it repels his evil. (Fath) 'By mutual consent': that the imam makes a truce with them for a fixed amount of dirhams, dinars, or other things. ('Ayni) 'One should not deviate from it': i.e., from the jizyah set by mutual consent and agreement, because it is determined by what is agreed upon. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/437] And the obligating factor is mutual consent, so it is not permissible to deviate from it to something else; and because he (peace be upon him) made a truce with the people of Najran for two thousand garments, half in Safar and half in Rajab. Najran is a region in Yemen. What is in al-Hidayah that he made a truce with Banu Najran for one thousand (garments) and two hundred garments is incorrect; the correct is two thousand garments. (Fath) 'Otherwise it is imposed, etc.': meaning if the jizyah is not imposed by mutual consent but by compulsion, i.e., when the imam overpowers the disbelievers and confirms them on their wealth, then it is imposed on the poor able-bodied person: that is, the healthy one capable of earning, at twelve dirhams per year, taken from him one dirham each month, so that even if he does not work despite being able, it is due, like the one who neglects kharaj land. And it is said: the poor able-bodied person is the one who can obtain dirhams and dinars by any means even if he does not know a craft. (Fath) The poor able-bodied person: he who earns more than his need; or it is said he who owns less than two hundred (dirhams). ('Ayni) And on the middle class double that [i.e., he who has wealth but is not independent of earning through his wealth. ('Ayni)] And know that consideration for wealth and poverty is over the majority of the year; so if he is wealthy for half the year and poor for half, the middle jizyah is taken from him. The wealthy is he who owns ten thousand dirhams or more – this is the best opinion and is relied upon. Abu Ja'far considered custom as the criterion. The middle person is he who owns from two hundred dirhams to ten thousand; it is also said he who has wealth but is not independent of earning. The poor is he who does not own two hundred dirhams; it is also said he who must earn to sustain his livelihood. The conclusion: when the imam overpowers the disbelievers and confirms them on their wealth... (text cut off)
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This section provides a comprehensive overview of jizyah in the Hanafi school. It begins by defining jizyah as a tax that suffices (tujzi') to prevent the killing of the dhimmi. The ruling is that if jizyah is set by mutual agreement (such as a peace treaty), that amount is binding and cannot be changed. If no agreement is made and the imam imposes it by force (after conquest), then the standard amounts are: 12 dirhams per year for a poor able-bodied person, paid monthly; double that for the middle class; and the wealthy are assessed more (though not specified here, the text later defines wealthy as owning 10,000 dirhams or more, which may be the basis). The evidence is Quran 9:29 and the Prophet's practice with the Zoroastrians of Najran. The commentary refutes an objection: jizyah is not a ransom for allowing disbelief but rather compensation for sparing life and property, analogous to diyah or qiṣāṣ. It also clarifies that jizyah is meant to protect the dhimmi and potentially lead him to Islam. The passage details the categories based on wealth: poor, middle, and rich, with definitions and the principle that the majority of the year determines the category. The text ends with a discussion of Ibn al-Humam's view on custom.

Key Terms
جزية (jizyah): A tax imposed on non-Muslim subjects (dhimmis) in an Islamic state in return for protection and exemption from military service.
ذمة (dhimmah): The covenant of protection granted to non-Muslims living under Islamic rule, implying rights and duties.
الفقير المعتمل (al-faqīr al-mu'tamal): A poor person who is physically able to work (even if not currently employed).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how much jizyah is imposed on a poor able-bodied dhimmi per year if no mutual agreement exists?
A) 12 dirhams per year, paid monthly
B) 24 dirhams per year
C) 10 dirhams per year
D) It depends on the imam's discretion

Key Takeaway
Jizyah is an annual obligation on able-bodied male dhimmis, with amounts varying by wealth category; its justification is compensation for protection, not a penalty for disbelief.
